Definition of Hamd and Its Conditions
Commentator defines hamd and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of hamd (praise) and distinguish its condition of voluntary goodness using the commentator's criteria.

Core Idea
Hamd is praise only for voluntary goodness, not for inherent qualities.

In this passage, the commentator provides a precise definition of 'hamd' (praise), emphasizing that it must involve the tongue and be directed at voluntary goodness. He uses a Qur'anic example to highlight the emphatic nature of the phrase, and distinguishes proper praise from mere compliments on non-voluntary attributes. Let's unpack this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the core definition
Find what is being defined and the basic definition. The term is 'al-hamd' and the definition is 'mentioning goodness with the tongue'.
The commentator explicitly states: 'al-hamd: it is the mention of goodness with the tongue.'
2
Understand the emphatic addition of 'with the tongue'
The commentator explains that 'with the tongue' is emphasized, similar to the Qur'anic verse 'Asra bi-'abdihi laylan'. This emphasizes that praise must be verbal, not just mental.
The repetition of 'with the tongue' is for emphasis (ta'kid).
3
Extract the condition of voluntary goodness
The definition specifies 'voluntary goodness' (al-khayr al-ikhtiyari). 'Voluntary' here means the quality or action is by the choice of the one praised, as understood customarily.
The commentator clarifies that 'voluntary' is based on custom, not strict philosophy. So if someone praises another for a quality not within their control, it is not hamd.
4
Distinguish hamd from mere compliment
The commentator gives the example of praising a pearl for its luster. This is not hamd because the luster is not voluntary. So, hamd is only for actions or qualities that are freely chosen.
This example shows how the definition excludes cases where the quality is inherent or non-voluntary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد وهو ذكر الخير باللسان، فذكر اللسان بعده مبني على التحرير كذكر الليل بعد أسرى في قوله تعالى: أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلا (الإسراء: ۱) والمراد بالثناء باللسان أعم من أن يصدر عنه، أو من جنس ما يصدر عنه سواء كان صادراً عنه أو لا، وحينئذ يندرج فيه حمده تعالى على ذاته وخلقه؛ لأنه من جنس القول. فتدبر. الاختياري للمحمود بخلاف الممدوح عليه؛ فإنه يجوز أن يكون غير اختياري أيضا مثل مدحت اللؤلؤ على صفائها. والمراد بالاختياري ما هو متفاهم العرف، وهو ما لا يكون باختيار الغير، وإن لم يكن اختياريا بالحقيقة، ولا التفات لأهل العرف إلى التدقيقات الحكمية. (عبد)
Al-hamd: it is the mention of goodness with the tongue. The mention of 'tongue' after it is based on emphasis, like the mention of 'night' after 'He took His servant by night' in His saying: 'Exalted is He who took His servant by night' (al-Isra 1). The intended meaning of praising with the tongue is general, whether it emanates from him or is of the same kind that emanates from him, whether it emanates or not. In that case, it includes His praise, exalted, upon His Essence and His creation, because it is of the kind of speech. So reflect. The goodness (al-khayr) is the voluntary goodness of the one praised, unlike that which is praised; for it is permissible that it be non-voluntary, such as my praise of a pearl for its luster. The intended meaning of 'voluntary' is what is understood customarily, i.e. that which is not by the choice of another, even if it is not voluntary in reality, and the people of custom do not pay attention to philosophical subtleties. ('Abd)
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'hamd' (praise) that the commentator elaborates on. He defines hamd as 'the mention of goodness with the tongue'. The phrase 'with the tongue' is emphasized, similar to the Qur'anic usage in 'Asra bi-'abdihi laylan' where 'laylan' (night) is mentioned for emphasis. The praise can be of two types: praise that the speaker himself utters, or praise of the same kind that could be uttered by someone else; it is general. This definition includes praise of God for His Essence and His creation, because it falls under speech. The key point is that the 'goodness' being praised must be voluntary (ikhtiyari) on the part of the one praised, meaning it stems from his choice. This excludes cases like praising a pearl for its luster, because the pearl's luster is not voluntary. The commentator clarifies that 'voluntary' is understood according to custom, not in the strict philosophical sense. So if someone praises another for a quality that is not within the praised person's control (like beauty), it is not considered hamd in the proper sense. This distinction is important in Islamic theology when discussing praise of God: God's actions and attributes are voluntary. The example of the pearl is from the Arabic tradition of praising objects.

Key Terms
الحمد
الاختياري
الثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is an example of proper hamd?
A) Praising a person for their voluntary charity
B) Praising a person for their noble lineage
C) Praising a sunset for its beauty
D) Praising a stone for its hardness

Key Takeaway
The definition of hamd requires verbal mention of voluntary goodness, as understood by custom, excluding praise for non-voluntary attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify whether each of the following is proper hamd according to the commentator's definition: praising a scholar for his voluntary teaching; praising a mountain for its height. Explain your reasoning.
?
2. Using the commentator's custom-based understanding of 'voluntary', examine the phrase 'alhamdulillahi rabbil 'alameen'. Why does this qualify as hamd?

The Multiple Definitions of Aal (آل النبي)
Author lists multiple opinions on Aal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and differentiate the various scholarly opinions on who constitutes the Aal of the Prophet as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
Who are the Aal? Scholars disagree: from infallible descendants to every pious person.

The term 'Aal' appears in many hadith and fiqh contexts, but its exact referent has been disputed. In this lesson, we explore the multiple definitions provided in the commentary, ranging from the Imami view of infallible descendants to the opinion that Aal includes all pious believers. Understanding these differences is crucial for applying rulings related to the Aal.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first definition
The passage begins: 'وآل النبي عترته المعصومون' (the family are his infallible descendants). Note that this is the Imami commentator's view.
2
Identify the additional opinions listed
After the verse, the text gives: قيل: بنو هاشم وبنو مطلب, وقيل: ذرية فاطمة, أو جميع القريش, أو أمة الإجابة, أو الأتقياء منهم. List each.
3
Evaluate the evidence for each opinion
The passage mentions a hadith with a weak chain for the 'pious' opinion. Al-Dawani favored it. Note the strength of evidence.
4
Compare the scope of each definition
Which is narrowest? Broadest? Think about implications for inclusion/exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وآل النبي عترته المعصومون قوله وأصحابه هم المؤمنون الذين أدركوا صحبة النبي عليا الإيمان قوله في مناهج : جمع منهج، وهو الطريق الواضح قوله الصدق: الخبر والاعتقاد إذا طابق الواقع كان الواقع أيضاً مطابقا له؛ فإن المفاعلة من الطرفين، فمن حيث أنه مطابق للواقع صدقا، ومن حيث أن الواقع مطابق له حقا، وقد يطلق الصدق - بالكسر - يسمى والحق على نفس المطابقة أيضاً قوله بالتصديق متعلق بقوله: سعدوا، أي بسبب التصديق والإيمان بما جاء به النبي عليه قوله: وصعدوا في معارج الحق يعني بلغوا أقصى مراتب الحق؛ فإن الصعود على جميع مراتبه يستلزم ذلك قوله بالتحقيق: ظرف لغو متعلق بـ"صعدوا" كما مر المعصومون: أي المحفوظون عن ارتكاب الصغائر والكبائر. قال الله تعالى في شأنهم: ﴿إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنْكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيران (الأحزاب:۳۳)، وهذا على مذهب الشارح؛ لأنه من الإمامية.(إسماعيل)، وفي الأنوار: آل النبي بنو هاشم وبنو مطلب أيضًا، وقيل: ذرية فاطمة الهما، كما رواه النووي، أو جميع القريش، أو أمة الإجابة، أو الأتقياء منهم، كما أخرج الطبراني بسند ضعيف "آل محمد كل تقي": والمحقق الدواني رجح الأخير. (عبد) صحبة النبي علي قليلاً كان أو كثيراً في حياته الصورية يقظة. (عبد)
And the family of the Prophet are his infallible descendants. His statement: 'and his companions' — they are the believers who attained the companionship of the Prophet upon faith. His statement: 'in the methodologies' — plural of 'manhaj', which is the clear path. His statement: 'truth' — the report and belief, if they correspond to reality, then reality also corresponds to them; because the mutual interaction is from both sides. Insofar as it corresponds to reality, it is called 'true' (sidq), and insofar as reality corresponds to it, it is called 'right' (haqq). Also, 'truth' (sidq) with a kasra is sometimes used, and 'right' is applied to the correspondence itself. His statement: 'by verification' relates to his statement: 'they were fortunate', i.e., due to the verification and faith in what the Prophet brought. His statement: 'and they ascended in the stations of truth' means they reached the highest levels of truth; for ascending through all its levels necessitates that. His statement: 'by investigation' is a redundant adverb related to 'they ascended' as before. 'The infallible' means those preserved from committing minor and major sins. Allah said concerning them: 'Indeed, Allah intends to remove from you impurity, O people of the house, and purify you with a thorough purification' (Al-Ahzab 33). This is according to the commentator's school because he is from the Imamiyyah. (Ismail) In Al-Anwar: The family of the Prophet are the Banu Hashim and Banu Muttalib as well. It is also said: the descendants of Fatima (the two? [؟]), as reported by Nawawi, or all of Quraysh, or the responding ummah, or the pious among them, as narrated by Tabarani with a weak chain: 'The family of Muhammad is every pious person.' And al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani favored the last. (Abd) Companionship of the Prophet, little or much, during his external life while awake. (Abd)
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage from the commentary (sharh) defines two key terms: 'Aal' (family) and 'Ashaab' (companions) of the Prophet. It begins by stating that 'Aal' are his infallible descendants, citing the verse of purification (33:33) to support infallibility, noting this is from the Imami perspective. Then it lists multiple scholarly opinions on who constitutes 'Aal': Banu Hashim and Banu Muttalib, descendants of Fatima, all Quraysh, the entire ummah of response, or the pious among them. The last opinion is strengthened by a hadith from Tabarani, though with a weak chain, and al-Dawani preferred it. For 'Ashaab' (companions), it defines them as believers who met the Prophet, regardless of duration, during his lifetime while awake. The passage also explains the meaning of 'manahij' (methodologies) as clear paths, 'sidq' (truth) as correspondence between report/belief and reality, and the relationship between 'sidq' and 'haqq'. It clarifies that 'tasdiq' (verification) is the cause of their fortune, and 'tas'ud' (ascension) in the stations of truth implies reaching the highest levels. The commentary follows the order of the original text, expanding on each phrase.

Key Terms
آل النبي
عترة معصومون
بنو هاشم
بنو مطلب
ذرية فاطمة
أمة الإجابة
الأتقياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT given as an opinion on who constitutes the Aal?
A) Banu Hashim and Banu Muttalib
B) The pious among the ummah
C) All of Quraysh
D) All prophets

Key Takeaway
The definition of Aal varies by school; the pious opinion is supported by a hadith but with a weak chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
From the passage, list the five distinct opinions on who constitutes the Aal. For each, identify whether it includes or excludes non-blood relatives.
?
Based on the hadith cited with a weak chain, assess why al-Dawani might have favored the last opinion (pious). What does this tell you about the role of isnad evaluation in juristic preferences?

Defining the Sahaba (الصحابة) – Genus and Differentia
Author defines Sahaba with genus and differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from the definition of Sahaba and evaluate whether it is a hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
A Sahaba is a believer who met the Prophet in this life, awake – a simple definition with broad implications.

The term 'Sahaba' is foundational for evaluating hadith transmission and authority. This lesson dissects the definition given in the commentary: 'the believers who attained the companionship of the Prophet upon faith.' By analyzing its components, you will understand the logical structure of definition and how it applies in hadith sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and the definition phrase
The text: 'وأصحابه هم المؤمنون الذين أدركوا صحبة النبي عليا الإيمان' (And his companions are the believers who attained the companionship of the Prophet upon faith).
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The broader category: المؤمنون (believers). So the genus is 'believers'.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
What distinguishes them from other believers: 'who attained the companionship of the Prophet upon faith' – includes meeting him in his lifetime while awake and remaining in faith.
4
Classify the definition type
Is this a complete real definition (hadd haqiqi) or a description (rasm)? Since it gives a functional description, it may be a rasm. Note that it does not mention a specific duration – any meeting qualifies.
5
Test the definition's completeness
Does it include all companions? Yes, even those who met briefly. Excludes those who met after his death or without faith. Does it exclude any who should be considered? Possibly those who met him but later apostatized? The phrase 'upon faith' implies they were believers at the time, but the definition does not explicitly require remaining in faith until death; however, the context of 'as-Sahaba' usually implies they died as Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وآل النبي عترته المعصومون قوله وأصحابه هم المؤمنون الذين أدركوا صحبة النبي عليا الإيمان قوله في مناهج : جمع منهج، وهو الطريق الواضح قوله الصدق: الخبر والاعتقاد إذا طابق الواقع كان الواقع أيضاً مطابقا له؛ فإن المفاعلة من الطرفين، فمن حيث أنه مطابق للواقع صدقا، ومن حيث أن الواقع مطابق له حقا، وقد يطلق الصدق - بالكسر - يسمى والحق على نفس المطابقة أيضاً قوله بالتصديق متعلق بقوله: سعدوا، أي بسبب التصديق والإيمان بما جاء به النبي عليه قوله: وصعدوا في معارج الحق يعني بلغوا أقصى مراتب الحق؛ فإن الصعود على جميع مراتبه يستلزم ذلك قوله بالتحقيق: ظرف لغو متعلق بـ"صعدوا" كما مر المعصومون: أي المحفوظون عن ارتكاب الصغائر والكبائر. قال الله تعالى في شأنهم: ﴿إِنَّمَا يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ لِيُذْهِبَ عَنْكُمُ الرِّجْسَ أَهْلَ الْبَيْتِ وَيُطَهِّرَكُمْ تَطْهِيران (الأحزاب:۳۳)، وهذا على مذهب الشارح؛ لأنه من الإمامية.(إسماعيل)، وفي الأنوار: آل النبي بنو هاشم وبنو مطلب أيضًا، وقيل: ذرية فاطمة الهما، كما رواه النووي، أو جميع القريش، أو أمة الإجابة، أو الأتقياء منهم، كما أخرج الطبراني بسند ضعيف "آل محمد كل تقي": والمحقق الدواني رجح الأخير. (عبد) صحبة النبي علي قليلاً كان أو كثيراً في حياته الصورية يقظة. (عبد)

Essential vs Accidental Accidents: The Role of Intermediaries in Occurrence
Commentary distinguishes 'arad bi al-dhat from 'arad bi al-'arad with examples of ta'ajjub and dahik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between an essential accident ('arad dhati) and an accidental accident ('arad 'aradi) by identifying whether an intermediary in occurrence (wasita fi al-'urud) is present, using examples from the commentary.

Core Idea
An accident is essential if it attaches without an intermediary; if it passes through an intermediary, it is accidental.

In Islamic logic, classifying accidents as essential or accidental is crucial for constructing correct definitions. For example, when defining 'human', wonder (ta'ajjub) is essential but laughter (dahik) is accidental because it attaches through the intermediary of being a wonderer. This lesson unpacks the commentary's explanation of this distinction and the role of intermediaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find the original division in the passage: accidents either attach primarily and essentially or through an intermediary. Note the Arabic phrases 'أوّلاً وبالذات' and 'بواسطة'.
The passage states this at the beginning.
2
Examine the first example: wonder
The passage says wonder attaches to man primarily and essentially, without any intermediary. Ask: Why is wonder essential for man? Because it stems from man's nature as a being capable of wonder.
The commentary emphasizes that wonder attaches to man insofar as he is man.
3
Examine the second example: laughter
Laughter attaches to the wonderer first, then is attributed to man accidentally. Identify the intermediary: the wonderer. Laughter is not directly attached to man.
The passage calls this 'عرض بالعرض والمجاز'.
4
Define essential and accidental accidents
From the analysis, define 'arad dhati as an accident that attaches without an intermediary in occurrence (wasita fi al-'urud), and 'arad 'aradi as one that attaches through an intermediary.
The commentary explicitly defines 'بالذات' as 'لا بواسطة شيء آخر' with reference to wasita fi al-'urud.

المقدمة الموضوع وأجزاء تعريف الموضوع إما أوّلاً وبالذات كالتعجب اللاحق للإنسان من حيث أنه إنسان، وإما بواسطة أمر مساوٍ لذلك الشيء، كالضحك الذي يعرض حقيقة للمتعجب ثم ينسب عروضه إلى الإنسان بالعرض والمجاز. فافهم. وبالذات أي لا بواسطة شيء آخر، والمراد بها ههنا الواسطة في العروض، وهو أن يعرض الشيء لشيء أولاً وحقيقة، وينسب إلى الشيء الآخر بالعرض، كحركة جالس السفينة، فإنها عارضة حقيقة للسفينة، وتنسب إلى الجالس بالعرض، وأحد قسمي الواسطة في الثبوت، وهو أن يعرض الشيء الواسطة والمعروض كليهما، لكن للواسطة أولاً ولذي الواسطة ثانياً، كحركة المفتاح بواسطة حركة اليد وكالتعجب اللاحق للإنسان من حيث أنه إنسان؛ فإنه عارض للإنسان بلا واسطة وكالصبغ العارض للثوب بواسطة الصباغ التي هي من ثاني قسمي الواسطة في الثبوت، وهو أن يعرض الشيء للشيء من غير عروضه للواسطة لكنها آلة محصنة، وأما بواسطة إلخ: المراد بـ'واسطة ههنا الواسطة في العروض، وأحد قسمي الواسطة في الثبوت المنفيين هناك كالضحك إلخ، ههنا واسطة في الثبوت بأحد قسميه إن كانت العروض بواسطة أعم، كالمشي العارض للإنسان بواسطة الحيوان، أو أخص، كالضحك العارض للحيوان بواسطة الناطق، أو أعم من وجه كتفريق البصر العارض للإنسان الأبيض، الذي هو واسطة يسمى العارض الكذائي عرضا غريبا والعارض للشيء بواسطة الجزء الأعم، اختلفوا في تسميته عرضا ذاتيا أو غريبا، والتفصيل في موضعه. (محصل) كالتعجب التعجب قد يطلق على إدراك أمر غريب خفي السبب، وهو المراد بالتعجب ههنا، فلا التعجب هو انفعال عرض للنفس عند إدراك أمور غريبة، فلا يلحق الإنسان لذاته بل لإدراك أمر يساويه، هو إدراك أمور غريبة ، فيكون التعجب حينئذ لاحقا للإنسان بواسطة أمر يساويه. أمر مساو: سواء كان جزءاً له أو خارجا عنه، كإدراك المعقولات اللاحقة للإنسان بواسطة أنه ناطق. (عبد) للمتعجب : أي المتعجب بالفعل مساو للإنسان؛ إذ لا يوجد فرد منه إلا وأن يكون متعجبا، فإنه يعرض للأطفال في المهد؛ ولذا يضحكون فإن قيل: إن الضحك ليس بعرض ذاتي للمتعجب؛ لأنه أخص منه، فالتعجب قد يكون سببا للبكاء، وقد يكون للفرح أو الغم أو الخوف مع أن العرض الذاتي لذي الواسطة يجب أن يكون عرضا ذاتيا لها أيضًا قلنا كون التعجب معروضا للضاحك بلا واسطة، وهو المقصود. فافهم لعله إشارة إلى الاختلاف في أن المعلول ينسب إلى العلل البعيدة المؤثرة أم لا، ولكن الأصح أنه ينسب (عبد) = یرد أن
The introduction: The subject and parts of the definition of the subject. Either primarily and essentially, like wonder attaching to man insofar as he is man, or through an intermediary coextensive with that thing, like laughter which attaches truly to the wonderer then its attachment is attributed to man accidentally and metaphorically. Understand. And 'essentially' means without an intermediary, and what is intended here is the intermediary in occurrence, which is that a thing attaches to a thing primarily and truly, and is attributed to the other thing accidentally, like the movement of the ship's passenger, for it truly attaches to the ship and is attributed to the passenger accidentally. And one of the two divisions of intermediary in subsistence, which is that the thing attaches to both the intermediary and the subject, but to the intermediary first and to the subject second, like the movement of the key through the movement of the hand, and like wonder attaching to man insofar as he is man, for it attaches to man without an intermediary, and like the dye attaching to the garment through the dyer, which is of the second division of intermediary in subsistence, which is that the thing attaches to a thing without attaching to the intermediary but it is an instrumental means. And as for 'through an intermediary' etc.: what is meant by 'intermediary' here is the intermediary in occurrence, and one of the two divisions of intermediary in subsistence that are negated there, like laughter etc. Here it is an intermediary in subsistence in one of its two divisions if the attachment is through a more general intermediary, like walking attaching to man through animal, or more specific, like laughter attaching to animal through rational, or overlapping, like the scattering of vision attaching to the white man, which is an intermediary called such an accident a strange accident. And the accident attaching to a thing through a more general part, they differed in naming it essential or strange accident, and the detail is in its place. (Muhassil) Like wonder: Wonder is sometimes applied to the perception of a strange matter whose cause is hidden, and that is what is intended by wonder here. So wonder is a passion that is an accident to the soul upon perceiving strange matters, so it does not attach to man in himself but due to the perception of a matter coextensive with him, which is the perception of strange matters. Then wonder at that time attaches to man through a coextensive matter. Coextensive matter: whether it is a part of him or external to him, like the perception of intelligibles attaching to man through his being rational. (Abd) To the wonderer: i.e., the actual wonderer is coextensive with man, for there is no individual of him except that he is a wonderer, for it attaches to children in the cradle; hence they laugh. If it is said: Laughter is not an essential accident for the wonderer because it is more specific, so wonder may be a cause for crying, and may be for joy or sadness or fear, while the essential accident of the subject must also be an essential accident for it. We say: The fact that wonder is a subject for the laugher without an intermediary is what is intended. Understand. Perhaps it is an indication to the disagreement about whether the effect is attributed to distant effective causes or not, but the correct is that it is attributed. (Abd) = it is said that
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary distinguishing two types of accident ('arad): essential (bi al-dhat) and accidental (bi al-'arad). The author begins by stating the classification: accidents attach either primarily and essentially, like wonder (ta'ajjub) attaching to man insofar as he is man, or through an intermediary, like laughter (dahik) which attaches to the wonderer then is attributed to man accidentally. The commentary then explains the term 'essentially' as without any intermediary in occurrence (wasita fi al-'urud). It contrasts this with the intermediary in subsistence (wasita fi al-thubut), which has two divisions. The first division is where both the intermediary and the subject receive the accident, but the intermediary receives it first and the subject second, like key movement through hand movement. The second division is where the accident attaches to the subject without attaching to the intermediary, which is merely an instrumental cause, like dye attaching to cloth through the dyer. The author then clarifies that 'intermediary' here refers to wasita fi al-'urud but sometimes also to one of the two divisions of wasita fi al-thubut. Further, examples are given to illustrate different logical relationships: walking attaches to man through the more general genus 'animal', laughter attaches to animal through the differentia 'rational', and the scattering of vision attaches to the white man through an overlapping intermediary. There is discussion about whether such accidents are called essential or strange (gharib). The commentary then delves into the meaning of wonder, defining it as a passion upon perceiving strange matters, and showing that it attaches to man through a coextensive matter. The dialectical exchange at the end addresses an objection: laughter is not an essential accident for the wonderer because wonder is more general than laughter, but the response is that wonder is a subject for laughter without an intermediary, and that is what matters. This passage guards against confusing the two types of accidents and the roles of intermediaries.

Key Terms
عرض ذاتي
عرض عرضي
واسطة في العروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes an essential accident ('arad dhati)?
A) An accident that attaches through an intermediary that is coextensive with the subject.
B) An accident that attaches to a thing primarily and essentially, without any intermediary.
C) An accident that attaches to a thing accidentally and metaphorically.
D) An accident that attaches through a more general genus.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essential and accidental accidents is fundamentally about the presence or absence of an intermediary in occurrence (wasita fi al-'urud).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify whether the following is an essential or accidental accident for the thing mentioned: 'The ability to learn' for 'human'. Use the criterion of intermediary in occurrence. Provide reasoning.
?
2. Construct a brief argument showing that 'writing' is an accidental accident for 'human' because it attaches through an intermediary (e.g., acquired skill).

Types of Intermediaries: Wasita fi al-'Urud and Wasita fi al-Thubut
Commentary defines wasita fi al-'urud and wasita fi al-thubut with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intermediary in occurrence (wasita fi al-'urud) and intermediary in subsistence (wasita fi al-thubut), and identify the two divisions of the latter.

Core Idea
An intermediary can affect how an accident is attributed (occurrence) or how it subsists in the subject (subsistence) — they are not the same.

The commentary not only distinguishes essential from accidental accidents but also delves into the nature of intermediaries. It contrasts an intermediary in occurrence (wasita fi al-'urud) with an intermediary in subsistence (wasita fi al-thubut), the latter having two types. Understanding this distinction is key to analyzing causal chains in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types of conditions
The passage introduces two types of 'bilā wasīṭa' (without intermediary) and 'bi wasīṭa' (with intermediary) in different senses: one in occurrence (al-'urūḍ) and one in subsistence (al-thubūt). Locate these terms in the Arabic.
Look for phrases like 'واسطة في العروض' and 'واسطة في الثبوت'.
2
Define wasita fi al-'urud
This is when an accident attaches primarily to one thing and then is attributed to another thing accidentally, e.g., the ship's movement attributed to the passenger. The intermediary is in the attribution, not the existence.
The passage gives the example of حركة جالس السفينة.
3
Define wasita fi al-thubut and its two divisions
This is when the accident subsists in the subject through a cause. Two types: (1) both intermediary and subject receive the accident, but the intermediary first, e.g., key movement through hand; (2) only the subject receives it, and the intermediary is an instrument, e.g., dye on cloth through dyer.
The passage lists these with the examples of حركة المفتاح and الصبغ.
4
Apply to wonder and laughter
The passage says wonder attaches to man without intermediary in occurrence, but laughter attaches through an intermediary (the wonderer). However, it then discusses whether laughter itself is a wasita fi al-thubut. Note that here the intermediary is in occurrence, not subsistence.
The commentary clarifies that the intermediary in occurrence is the primary focus for the essential/accidental distinction.

Commentary

The author begins by stating the classification: accidents attach either primarily and essentially, like wonder (ta'ajjub) attaching to man insofar as he is man, or through an intermediary, like laughter (dahik) which attaches to the wonderer then is attributed to man accidentally. The first division is where both the intermediary and the subject receive the accident, but the intermediary receives it first and the subject second, like key movement through hand movement. The second division is where the accident attaches to the subject without attaching to the intermediary, which is merely an instrumental cause, like dye attaching to cloth through the dyer. The author then clarifies that 'intermediary' here refers to wasita fi al-'urud but sometimes also to one of the two divisions of wasita fi al-thubut. Further, examples are given to illustrate different logical relationships: walking attaches to man through the more general genus 'animal', laughter attaches to animal through the differentia 'rational', and the scattering of vision attaches to the white man through an overlapping intermediary. According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the first division of wasita fi al-thubut (where both intermediary and subject receive the accident)?

Key Takeaway
Intermediaries in occurrence affect attribution, while intermediaries in subsistence affect the ontological grounding of an accident in its subject.

The Threefold Division of Conventional Signification
Author divides linguistic signification into three types: مطابقة, تضمن, التزام.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conventional signification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and explain why 'tamam' is preferred over 'jami' for the whole meaning.

Core Idea
Every conventional word signifies either the whole, a part, or a concomitant of its meaning.

How does a word convey its meaning? This lesson unpacks the foundational logical classification of linguistic signification. You will learn the three modes—matching, inclusion, and implication—and see why the logician chooses the term 'tamam' (completeness) over 'jami' (totality) for the entire assigned meaning. This classification is not arbitrary but rationally exhaustive, as the commentary demonstrates.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three categories from the matn
Read the first Arabic sentence: 'لأن دلالة اللفظ بسبب وضع الواضع إما على تمام الموضوع له، أو على جزئه، أو على أمر خارج عنه.' Identify that the three categories are: (1) upon the whole (mutabaqa), (2) upon a part (tadammun), (3) upon an external concomitant (iltizam).
The matn uses 'تمام الموضوع له' for the whole meaning.
2
Understand the commentator's terminological choice
The commentary explains why the author chose 'تمام' (tamam) over 'جميع' (jami): 'jami' suggests multiplicity and composition, whereas 'tamam' can apply to simple concepts like the Necessary Being. Recognize that 'tamam' contrasts with 'naqs' (deficiency) and 'jami' contrasts with 'ba'd' (partiality).
Example: The Necessary Being is called 'تمام الوجود' but not 'جميع الوجود'.
3
Analyze the rational exhaustive division
The commentary states that the division is 'rational' and reduces to a disjunction: either the signification is upon the entire meaning or not; if not, it is either upon a part or upon something external. This shows that the three categories are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.
This is a logical classification, not a mere list.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the word 'salat' in the Qur'an. By mutabaqa it signifies the entire act of prayer; by tadammun it signifies a part (e.g., bowing); by iltizam it signifies the reward or the prohibition of indecency. Practice identifying which signification is intended in a given verse.
This skill is essential for precise understanding of legal texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن دلالة اللفظ بسبب وضع الواضع إما على تمام الموضوع له، أو على جزئه، أو على أمر خارج عنه. قوله إما على إنما اختار المصنف لفظ 'التمام' دون 'الجميع'؛ لأن الثاني مشعر بالتركيب بخلاف الأول، مع أن دلالة المفرد على معناه أيضًا مطابقة وجه الفرق بينهما : أن التمام ليس من شرطه أن يحيط بالكثرة بالقوة أو بالفعل، ولذا يقال للواجب إنه تمام الوجود بخلاف الجميع؛ فإنه مشروط بالإحاطة بالكثرة، فالتمام مقابل للنقص والجميع مقابل للبعض هكذا يفهم من كلمات الشيخ في الشفاء. اعلم أن حصر الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية في المطابقة والتضمن والالتزام عقلي، فإنه إن لم يكن دائرا بين النفي والإثبات إلا أنه راجع إليه بأن يقال: إن الدلالة على تمام الموضوع له أو لا، الأول مطابقة، والثاني لا يخلو إما أن يكون دلالة على جزء الموضوع له أو لا، الأول تضمن، والثاني التزام.
Because the signification of a word by the act of the poser is either upon the entirety of what it is assigned to, or upon a part of it, or upon an external thing. His saying 'either upon': indeed the author chose the term 'completeness' (al-tamam) instead of 'totality' (al-jami') because the latter suggests composition unlike the former, even though the signification of a simple term upon its meaning is also matching (mutabaqa); the difference between them is that 'completeness' does not require encompassing multiplicity potentially or actually, hence the Necessary Being is called 'complete existence' unlike 'totality'; for it requires encompassing multiplicity, so 'completeness' is opposite to deficiency and 'totality' is opposite to partiality. This is understood from the words of the Shaykh in al-Shifa. Know that the restriction of conventional linguistic signification to matching (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam) is rational. For even if it is not a direct disjunction between affirmation and negation, it reduces to it by saying: the signification is either upon the entirety of what it is assigned to or not; the first is matching; the second is either signification upon a part of what it is assigned to or not; the first is inclusion, the second is implication.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known threefold division of signification: مطابقة (matching), تضمن (inclusion), and التزام (implication). The author first reproduces the matn that every signification by convention (wad') is either upon the complete meaning (تمام الموضوع له), a part of it (جزئه), or an external concomitant (أمر خارج عنه). Then the commentary explains why the author chose the word 'تمام' (completeness) over 'جميع' (totality): 'تمام' does not imply composition and can apply to simple concepts, while 'جميع' suggests plurality. For example, the Necessary Being is called 'تمام الوجود' but not 'جميع الوجود' because He is not composed of parts. The commentary also provides a rational justification for the exhaustive division: it reduces to a disjunction of whether the signification is upon the whole meaning or not; if not, then it's either upon a part (inclusion) or upon something external (implication). This classification is foundational in logic for understanding how words relate to their meanings.

Key Terms
المطابقة
التضمن
الالتزام
التمام

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author choose the word 'تمام' (completeness) instead of 'جميع' (totality) to refer to the whole meaning?
A) Because 'تمام' is simpler and easier to understand.
B) Because 'جميع' suggests multiplicity and composition, unlike 'تمام' which can apply even to simple concepts like the Necessary Being.
C) Because 'تمام' means the part while 'جميع' means the whole.
D) Because the author followed the terminology of Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
Conventional signification is rationally divided into three exhaustive types: matching, inclusion, and implication, and the term 'تمام' is chosen over 'جميع' to avoid implying composition.

Compound and Single Expressions: The Four Conditions
Author defines compound and single with four conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic expression as compound or single by verifying the four conditions presented in the passage.

Core Idea
A compound requires parts, meaning parts, indication, and intentionality.

In Islamic logic, all expressions (alfaz) are divided into compound (murakkab) and single (mufrad). A compound expression is one where part of its wording indicates part of its meaning, and this indication is intended. The passage from Tahzeeb al-Mantiq lays out exactly four conditions that must all be met for an expression to be compound. By understanding these conditions, you will be able to analyze any expression and determine its type.

Guided Path
1
List the four conditions of a compound expression.
Find the four conditions: (1) the expression has parts, (2) its meaning has parts, (3) part of its wording indicates part of its meaning, (4) the indication is intended. These are all necessary.
2
Identify the four types of single expressions.
Each type corresponds to the absence of one condition. Match each condition to its counterexample: no part in wording (hamza), no part in meaning (Allah), no indication (proper names like Zayd), indication not intended ('rational animal' as proper name).
3
Apply the conditions to a new expression.
Take an expression like 'غلام زيد' (ghulam Zayd). Check: it has parts (غلام and زيد), its meaning has parts (servitude and Zayd), part of wording indicates part of meaning (غلام indicates servitude, زيد indicates Zayd), and the indication is intended. Therefore it is compound.
Remember that if any condition is absent, the expression is single, not compound.

(عبد) أن عنه، لم يحتملها أي إن لم يحتمل كلا من الصدق والكذب؛ فإن مدار الصدق والكذب على الحكاية عن المحكي وفي الإنشاء ليست الحكاية فأنّى الاحتمال . محصل (بزيادة للأول: المراد بالأول والثاني بحسب الرتبة لا بحسب اللفظ، فيشمل ما قدم فيه القيد على المقيد لفظا، نحو: راكبا جاء في عمرو ؛ فإن الحال قيد لعاملها قطعا ولكنه قد يؤخر عنها. (عبد) الدار : إشارة إلى ما اشتهر في ما بينهم من أن المركب التقييدي في الإضافي والتوصيفي منقوض بأمثال هذا المركب التقييدي، وقد عرفت أن ذا الحال مقيد أيضًا مركب تقييدي وليس منهما والجواب: مرادهم حصر المركب الكاسب والمكتسب في الإضافي والتوصيفي، مثل: حيوان ناطق وعدم البصر، والمركبات المذكورة في النقض ليست بكاسبة ولا مكتسبة، نحو: في الدار. فإن قيل: إن كلمة "في" لكونها حرفا لا تدل على معناها بدون ضم كلمة أخرى، فمعناه وهي الظرفية جزئي لا كلي حتى يصح تقييده وتخصيصه بالدار وغيرها فالدار محصلة ومقومة لمعناها. ولما كان وجود معناها بعد الدار لا أن يقال: إن معناها صار مقيدا بالدار، يصح فليس الجزء الثاني في الدار قيدا للجزء الأول، فالمثال مطابق بالممثل فافهم وكن من الشاكرين. (عبد) يكفي في التمثيل لفظ الدار فقط؛ فإنها أيضًا مركبة من حرف اللام واسم الدار وليس الثاني قيدا للأول. ( إسماعيل)
Concepts: either complete [which is] a statement, or incomplete. The compound: a word of which part of its meaning is [indicated] by part of its wording, then it is compound; otherwise it is single. The compound is realized by four matters: first, that it has a part; second, that its meaning has a part; third, that part of its wording indicates part of its meaning; fourth, that this indication is intended. So by the absence of each of the four conditions, the single is realized. Thus the compound has one division, and the single has four divisions: first, what has no part in wording, like the interrogative hamza; second, what has no part in its meaning, like the word 'Allah'; third, what has no indication of part of its wording on part of its meaning, like Zayd and 'Abdullah as proper names; fourth, what its wording indicates part of its meaning but the indication is not intended, like 'rational animal' as a proper name for the human person. His saying: 'either complete' means that it is self-contained, like 'Zayd is standing'. His saying: 'statement' if it admits truth and falsehood. Part of the single: He presented the compound before the single even though it is prior in nature, so it must be prior in convention to match convention with nature; because the opposition between single and compound is opposition of privation and possession, and the compound is possession, and privations are only known through their possessions, so knowledge of the single is dependent on knowledge of the compound, so it must precede it. (Ismail) The four: for these four matters are like constituents of the compound, so wherever one of these four is not realized, the compound is not realized, so the single must be realized, otherwise the enumeration here would be invalid. (Ismail) The single: because the concept of compound is restricted, and the removal of the restricted is realized by removing one of its restrictions or removing all restrictions. 'As proper names' — he only said 'proper names' because on the assumption of non-proper-name status, it is a relational compound; for part of its wording, like 'abd' for example, indicates part of the compositional meaning intended on this assumption, which is servitude. 'For the person' — i.e., the human essence, for individuation; for the meaning of 'animal' is part of it, but this indication is not intended in the case of a proper name, rather what is intended is the indication of the composite 'rational animal' on this individual meaning. 'Self-contained' — for if it is said: the transitive verb with its subject, like 'Zayd struck' for example, is a complete compound even though it is not self-contained, but needs mention of the object. I say: what is meant by 'self-contained' is that it does not need another thing like the need of the predicate for the subject and vice versa, and there is no doubt that such need is not present in the transitive verb with its subject, because it is [composed of] subject and predicate. Concepts [page 41] The compound: either a statement or a construction, and either incomplete: restrictive or other. 'Statement' — i.e., it is capable of being characterized by them [truth and falsehood] such that it is said to be true or false. His saying: 'or construction' — if it does not admit them. His saying: 'or incomplete' — if it is not self-contained. His saying: 'restrictive' — if the second part is a restriction of the first, like 'slave of Zayd', 'virtuous man', and 'standing in the house'. His saying: 'or other' — if the second is not a restriction of the first. He defined 'statement' first as admitting truth and falsehood as is well-known, and interpreted it in a way that repels the objection raised against it. The detail: that this definition is not comprehensive because it excludes statements that admit truth only and not falsehood, like our saying: 'God is our God' and 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God', or the opposite, like our saying: 'the earth is above us and the sky is beneath us'; for they do not admit truth and falsehood. And the answer that the conjunctive 'and' here means the disjunctive 'or' is nothing; for then it would need the word 'admit' to be added, as is not hidden. The way of repelling it by what the commentator interpreted is apparent; for what is meant by 'admitting truth and falsehood' is that the statement is such that it can be characterized by them, such that it is characterized in some instances by truth and in some by falsehood. So the essence of the statement in itself admits truth and falsehood; for the essence of the statement is the concept of our saying 'this is that', even if some instances are incapable of being characterized by one of them with respect to the particularity of the matter. This is what Al-Sayyid Al-Sanad Al-Sharif (may God sanctify his soul) verified. 'For it' — i.e., for the complete statemental compound: true or false, indicating that the type of statement, even if it is characterized by truth and falsehood, yet every individual instance is in actual fact either characterized by truth only or by falsehood. So understand and memorize. ('Abd) 'That' — 'about him', 'did not admit them' — i.e., if it does not admit both truth and falsehood; for the basis of truth and falsehood is narration about the narrated, and in construction there is no narration, so how can there be admission? Summarized. (With addition) 'For the first' — what is meant by 'first' and 'second' is according to rank, not according to wording, so it includes what the restriction is presented before the restricted in wording, like 'riding' came [in] 'Amr'; for the state is a restriction of its operator certainly, but it may be delayed from it. ('Abd) 'The house' — indication of what is well-known among them that the restrictive compound in the relational and descriptive is invalidated by examples like this restrictive compound, and you have known that the possessor of a state is also restrictive compound and is not one of them. The answer: what they mean is the enumeration of the 'gainful' and 'acquired' compound into relational and descriptive, like 'rational animal' and 'lack of sight', and the compounds mentioned in the objection are neither gainful nor acquired, like 'in the house'. For if it is said: the word 'in', being a particle, does not indicate its meaning without attachment to another word, so its meaning, which is adverbiality, is particular not universal such that it can be restricted and specified by 'house' and others, so 'house' is a constituent and a constituent of its meaning. And since the existence of its meaning comes after 'house', it is not said that its meaning became restricted by 'house', so the second part in 'in the house' is not a restriction of the first part, so the example matches what is exemplified. So understand and be among the thankful. ('Abd) It suffices in exemplification the word 'house' alone; for it is also composed of the letter 'lam' and the noun 'house', and the second is not a restriction of the first. (Ismail)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of expressions (alfaz) in the context of Tasawwurat (concepts). The author begins by stating that concepts are either complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis). He then defines the compound (murakkab) expression: a word where part of its wording indicates part of its meaning; otherwise it is single (mufrad). The compound is realized by four conditions: (1) the expression has parts, (2) its meaning has parts, (3) part of the wording indicates part of the meaning, and (4) this indication is intended. The absence of any condition yields a specific type of single expression. The author enumerates four types of single expressions: (i) no part in wording (e.g., hamzat al-istifham), (ii) no part in meaning (e.g., 'Allah'), (iii) no indication of part of wording on part of meaning (e.g., proper names like Zayd), and (iv) indication but not intended (e.g., 'rational animal' as a proper name for a human). The commentary then explains why the compound is discussed before the single: because the single is known only through the compound, like privation through possession. The marginal gloss (Ismail) clarifies that the four conditions are constitutive of the compound. The text then addresses an objection regarding transitive verbs (e.g., 'Zayd struck') being complete but not self-contained; the response is that self-containedness means not needing a separate subject or predicate, not the object of a transitive verb. Next, the author divides the complete compound into statement (khabar) and construction (insha'), and the incomplete compound into restrictive (taqyidi) and other. The commentator then discusses a well-known problem with the definition of khabar as 'admitting truth and falsehood': examples like 'God is our God' admit only truth, and 'the earth is above us' admit only falsehood. The solution, attributed to al-Sayyid al-Sanad al-Sharif, is that the definition considers the essence of khabar, which in itself potentially admits both, though particular instances may be restricted by their matter. The passage ends with a further refinement distinguishing between the type (which admits both) and the individual (which is actually true or false). The final marginalia discuss nuances of restrictive compounds, such as the example 'in the house' where the particle 'in' requires a noun to complete its meaning, and whether it counts as restrictive. The overall aim is to precisely define and classify expressions, guarding against misunderstandings about what qualifies as compound and how statements are defined.

Key Takeaway
A compound expression must satisfy four conditions; the single is defined by the absence of any one of them.

Complete and Incomplete Compounds: Khabar and Insha'
Author defines complete/incomplete and khabar/insha' with a solution to a definitional problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a compound expression as complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis), and within complete, as khabar (statement) or insha' (construction).

Core Idea
A complete compound is self-contained; a khabar admits truth/falsehood in essence, an insha' does not.

After distinguishing compound from single, the passage further divides compound expressions into complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis). A complete compound is one that is self-contained (yasihhu al-sukut 'alayhi), like a full proposition. If it is self-contained, it is either a statement (khabar) that can be true or false, or a construction (insha') that cannot, like commands and questions. This classification is essential for understanding how propositions function in Islamic logic and theology. The passage also addresses a definitional problem: statements that are necessarily true or false seem to violate the definition, but the solution is that the definition refers to the essence of khabar, which potentially admits both truth values, even if particular instances are restricted by their matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion of self-containedness.
A compound is complete if it does not need another part beyond the subject and predicate. For example, 'Zayd is standing' is complete. A transitive verb like 'Zayd struck' is also complete because it only needs subject and predicate, not the object, for self-containedness.
The passage clarifies that the need for an object does not make a sentence incomplete; only the need for another subject or predicate does.
2
Distinguish complete from incomplete.
An incomplete compound is not self-contained, like 'slave of Zayd' (غلام زيد) which needs something else to be a full statement. The passage splits incomplete into restrictive (taqyidi) and non-restrictive.
3
Identify khabar and insha' within complete.
If the complete compound admits truth and falsehood in essence, it is khabar. If it does not, it is insha'. For example, 'Stand up!' is insha' because it is not a statement that can be true or false.
4
Address the problem of necessary truth/falsehood.
The passage discusses cases like 'God is our God' which are always true. The solution: the definition considers the essence of khabar, which potentially admits both, though particular instances may be restricted by their matter. So khabar is defined by its capacity, not by actual truth-value.
This refinement comes from al-Sayyid al-Sharif.

التصورات إما تام خبر · 2. المركب للفظ على جزء معناه فهو المركب، وإلا فهو المفرد. فالمركب إنما يتحقق بأمور أربع الأول: أن يكون جزء، والثاني: أن يكون لمعناه جزء، والثالث: أن يدل جزء لفظه على جزء معناه، والرابع: أن تكون هذه الدلالة مرادة، فبانتفاء كل من القيود الأربعة يتحقق المفرد، فللمركب قسم واحد وللمفرد أقسام أربعة: الأول: ما لا جزء للفظ، نحو: همزة الاستفهام والثاني: ما لا جزء لمعناه، نحو: لفظ "الله" والثالث: ما لا دلالة لجزء لفظه على جزء معناه، كزيد وعبد الله علماً، والرابع: ما يدل لفظه على جزء معناه لكن الدلالة غير مقصودة، كالحيوان الناطق علماً للشخص الإنساني. قوله: إما تام أي يصح السكوت عليه كزيد قائم قوله :خبر إن احتمل الصدق والكذب، جزء المفرد: قدم المركب على المفرد مع أنه مقدم عليه طبعا، فلابد من تقدمه وضعا ليطابق الوضع الطبع؛ لأن التقابل بين المفرد والمركب تقابل العدم والملكة والمركب ملكة والإعدام إنما تعرف بملكاتها، فمعرفة المفرد موقوفة على معرفة المركب، فلابد من تقدمه عليه. ( إسماعيل) الأربعة فإن هذه الأمور الأربعة كالمقومات للمركب فكلما لا يتحقق واحد من هذه الأربعة لا يتحقق المركب، فلابد من تحقق المفرد وإلا لبطل الحصر ههنا. إسماعيل) المفرد : لأن مفهوم المركب مقيد، ورفع المقيد يتحقق برفع واحد من قيوده أو يرفع جميع القيود. علما إنما قال علما؛ لأنه على تقدير عدم العلمية مركب إضافي؛ فإن جزء لفظه كعبد مثلا، دليل على جزء المعنى التركيبي المقصود على هذا التقدير، وهو العبودية. للشخص أي الماهية الإنسانية للتشخص؛ فإن معنى الحيوان جزء لها، لكن هذه الدلالة ليست مقصودة في حالة العلمية، بل المقصود هو دلالة مجموع الحيوان الناطق على هذا المعنى الشخصي. يصح: فإن قيل: إن الفعل المتعدي مع الفاعل نحو: ضرب زيد مثلاً مركب تام مع أنه لا يصح السكوت عليه، بل يحتاج إلى ذكر المفعول. قلت: المراد من صحة السكوت عليه أن لا يحتاج إلى شيء آخر كاحتياج المحكوم عليه إلى المحكوم به وبالعكس، ولا شك أن مثل هذا الاحتياج ليس في الفعل المتعدي مع الفاعل؛ لأنه بالمسند والمسند إليه. التصورات ٤١ المركب أو إنشاء، وإما ناقص تقييدي أو غيره أي يكون من شأنه أن يتصف بهما بأن يقال له صادق أو كاذب. قوله: أو إنشاء: إن لم يحتملهما. قوله وإما ناقص إن لم يصح السكوت عليه قوله تقييدي: إن كان الجزء الثاني قيدا للأول، نحو: غلام زيد ورجل فاضل وقائم في الدار :قوله أو غيره إن لم يكن الثاني قيدا للأول، شأنه عرف الخبر أولاً بما يحتمل الصدق والكذب كما هو المشهور، وفسره بوجه يندفع منه الإشكال الوارد عليه، وتفصيله: أن هذا التعريف غير جامع؛ لأنه يخرج منه الأخبار التي تحتمل الصدق فقط دون الكذب، كقولنا: الله إلهنا ومحمد رسول الله أو بالعكس، كقولنا : الأرض فوقنا والسماء تحتنا؛ فإنها لا تحتمل الصدق والكذب، والجواب بأن الواو الواصلة ههنا بمعنى أو الفاصلة ليس بشيء؛ فإنه يستدرك حينئذ لفظ يحتمل، كما لا يخفى وجه الدفع بما فسره الشارح ظاهر؛ فإن المراد من احتمال الصدق والكذب أن يكون من شأن الخبر الاتصاف بهما بأن يتصف في بعض الأفراد بالصدق وفي بعضها بالكذب، فماهية الخبر من حيث ذاتها تحتمل الصدق والكذب؛ فإن ماهية الخبر هو مفهوم قولنا هذا ذاك وإن كان بعض الأفراد ممتنع الاتصاف بأحدهما بالنظر إلى خصوصية المادة. هكذا حقق السيد السند الشريف قدس سره. له: أي للمركب التام الخبري صادق أو كاذب إشارة إلى أن نوع الخبر وإن اتصف بالصدق والكذب لكن كل فرد من أفراد يكون في نفس الأمر إما متصفا بالصدق فقط أو بالكذب. فافهم واحفظ. (عبد) أن عنه، لم يحتملها أي إن لم يحتمل كلا من الصدق والكذب؛ فإن مدار الصدق والكذب على الحكاية عن المحكي وفي الإنشاء ليست الحكاية فأنّى الاحتمال . محصل (بزيادة للأول: المراد بالأول والثاني بحسب الرتبة لا بحسب اللفظ، فيشمل ما قدم فيه القيد على المقيد لفظا، نحو: راكبا جاء في عمرو ؛ فإن الحال قيد لعاملها قطعا ولكنه قد يؤخر عنها. (عبد) الدار : إشارة إلى ما اشتهر في ما بينهم من أن المركب التقييدي في الإضافي والتوصيفي منقوض بأمثال هذا المركب التقييدي، وقد عرفت أن ذا الحال مقيد أيضًا مركب تقييدي وليس منهما والجواب: مرادهم حصر المركب الكاسب والمكتسب في الإضافي والتوصيفي، مثل: حيوان ناطق وعدم البصر، والمركبات المذكورة في النقض ليست بكاسبة ولا مكتسبة، نحو: في الدار. فإن قيل: إن كلمة "في" لكونها حرفا لا تدل على معناها بدون ضم كلمة أخرى، فمعناه وهي الظرفية جزئي لا كلي حتى يصح تقييده وتخصيصه بالدار وغيرها فالدار محصلة ومقومة لمعناها. ولما كان وجود معناها بعد الدار لا أن يقال: إن معناها صار مقيدا بالدار، يصح فليس الجزء الثاني في الدار قيدا للجزء الأول، فالمثال مطابق بالممثل فافهم وكن من الشاكرين. (عبد) يكفي في التمثيل لفظ الدار فقط؛ فإنها أيضًا مركبة من حرف اللام واسم الدار وليس الثاني قيدا للأول. ( إسماعيل)
Concepts: either complete [which is] a statement, or incomplete. The compound: a word of which part of its meaning is [indicated] by part of its wording, then it is compound; otherwise it is single. The compound is realized by four matters: first, that it has a part; second, that its meaning has a part; third, that part of its wording indicates part of its meaning; fourth, that this indication is intended. So by the absence of each of the four conditions, the single is realized. Thus the compound has one division, and the single has four divisions: first, what has no part in wording, like the interrogative hamza; second, what has no part in its meaning, like the word 'Allah'; third, what has no indication of part of its wording on part of its meaning, like Zayd and 'Abdullah as proper names; fourth, what its wording indicates part of its meaning but the indication is not intended, like 'rational animal' as a proper name for the human person. His saying: 'either complete' means that it is self-contained, like 'Zayd is standing'. His saying: 'statement' if it admits truth and falsehood. Part of the single: He presented the compound before the single even though it is prior in nature, so it must be prior in convention to match convention with nature; because the opposition between single and compound is opposition of privation and possession, and the compound is possession, and privations are only known through their possessions, so knowledge of the single is dependent on knowledge of the compound, so it must precede it. (Ismail) The four: for these four matters are like constituents of the compound, so wherever one of these four is not realized, the compound is not realized, so the single must be realized, otherwise the enumeration here would be invalid. (Ismail) The single: because the concept of compound is restricted, and the removal of the restricted is realized by removing one of its restrictions or removing all restrictions. 'As proper names' — he only said 'proper names' because on the assumption of non-proper-name status, it is a relational compound; for part of its wording, like 'abd' for example, indicates part of the compositional meaning intended on this assumption, which is servitude. 'For the person' — i.e., the human essence, for individuation; for the meaning of 'animal' is part of it, but this indication is not intended in the case of a proper name, rather what is intended is the indication of the composite 'rational animal' on this individual meaning. 'Self-contained' — for if it is said: the transitive verb with its subject, like 'Zayd struck' for example, is a complete compound even though it is not self-contained, but needs mention of the object. I say: what is meant by 'self-contained' is that it does not need another thing like the need of the predicate for the subject and vice versa, and there is no doubt that such need is not present in the transitive verb with its subject, because it is [composed of] subject and predicate. Concepts [page 41] The compound: either a statement or a construction, and either incomplete: restrictive or other. 'Statement' — i.e., it is capable of being characterized by them [truth and falsehood] such that it is said to be true or false. His saying: 'or construction' — if it does not admit them. His saying: 'or incomplete' — if it is not self-contained. His saying: 'restrictive' — if the second part is a restriction of the first, like 'slave of Zayd', 'virtuous man', and 'standing in the house'. His saying: 'or other' — if the second is not a restriction of the first. He defined 'statement' first as admitting truth and falsehood as is well-known, and interpreted it in a way that repels the objection raised against it. The detail: that this definition is not comprehensive because it excludes statements that admit truth only and not falsehood, like our saying: 'God is our God' and 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God', or the opposite, like our saying: 'the earth is above us and the sky is beneath us'; for they do not admit truth and falsehood. And the answer that the conjunctive 'and' here means the disjunctive 'or' is nothing; for then it would need the word 'admit' to be added, as is not hidden. The way of repelling it by what the commentator interpreted is apparent; for what is meant by 'admitting truth and falsehood' is that the statement is such that it can be characterized by them, such that it is characterized in some instances by truth and in some by falsehood. So the essence of the statement in itself admits truth and falsehood; for the essence of the statement is the concept of our saying 'this is that', even if some instances are incapable of being characterized by one of them with respect to the particularity of the matter. This is what Al-Sayyid Al-Sanad Al-Sharif (may God sanctify his soul) verified. 'For it' — i.e., for the complete statemental compound: true or false, indicating that the type of statement, even if it is characterized by truth and falsehood, yet every individual instance is in actual fact either characterized by truth only or by falsehood. So understand and memorize. ('Abd) 'That' — 'about him', 'did not admit them' — i.e., if it does not admit both truth and falsehood; for the basis of truth and falsehood is narration about the narrated, and in construction there is no narration, so how can there be admission? Summarized. (With addition) 'For the first' — what is meant by 'first' and 'second' is according to rank, not according to wording, so it includes what the restriction is presented before the restricted in wording, like 'riding' came [in] 'Amr'; for the state is a restriction of its operator certainly, but it may be delayed from it. ('Abd) 'The house' — indication of what is well-known among them that the restrictive compound in the relational and descriptive is invalidated by examples like this restrictive compound, and you have known that the possessor of a state is also restrictive compound and is not one of them. The answer: what they mean is the enumeration of the 'gainful' and 'acquired' compound into relational and descriptive, like 'rational animal' and 'lack of sight', and the compounds mentioned in the objection are neither gainful nor acquired, like 'in the house'. For if it is said: the word 'in', being a particle, does not indicate its meaning without attachment to another word, so its meaning, which is adverbiality, is particular not universal such that it can be restricted and specified by 'house' and others, so 'house' is a constituent and a constituent of its meaning. And since the existence of its meaning comes after 'house', it is not said that its meaning became restricted by 'house', so the second part in 'in the house' is not a restriction of the first part, so the example matches what is exemplified. So understand and be among the thankful. ('Abd) It suffices in exemplification the word 'house' alone; for it is also composed of the letter 'lam' and the noun 'house', and the second is not a restriction of the first. (Ismail)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition of expressions (alfaz) in the context of Tasawwurat (concepts). The author begins by stating that concepts are either complete (tamm) or incomplete (naqis). He then defines the compound (murakkab) expression: a word where part of its wording indicates part of its meaning; otherwise it is single (mufrad). The compound is realized by four conditions: (1) the expression has parts, (2) its meaning has parts, (3) part of the wording indicates part of the meaning, and (4) this indication is intended. The absence of any condition yields a specific type of single expression. The author enumerates four types of single expressions: (i) no part in wording (e.g., hamzat al-istifham), (ii) no part in meaning (e.g., 'Allah'), (iii) no indication of part of wording on part of meaning (e.g., proper names like Zayd), and (iv) indication but not intended (e.g., 'rational animal' as a proper name for a human). The commentary then explains why the compound is discussed before the single: because the single is known only through the compound, like privation through possession. The marginal gloss (Ismail) clarifies that the four conditions are constitutive of the compound. The text then addresses an objection regarding transitive verbs (e.g., 'Zayd struck') being complete but not self-contained; the response is that self-containedness means not needing a separate subject or predicate, not the object of a transitive verb. Next, the author divides the complete compound into statement (khabar) and construction (insha'), and the incomplete compound into restrictive (taqyidi) and other. The commentator then discusses a well-known problem with the definition of khabar as 'admitting truth and falsehood': examples like 'God is our God' admit only truth, and 'the earth is above us' admit only falsehood. The solution, attributed to al-Sayyid al-Sanad al-Sharif, is that the definition considers the essence of khabar, which in itself potentially admits both, though particular instances may be restricted by their matter. The passage ends with a further refinement distinguishing between the type (which admits both) and the individual (which is actually true or false). The final marginalia discuss nuances of restrictive compounds, such as the example 'in the house' where the particle 'in' requires a noun to complete its meaning, and whether it counts as restrictive. The overall aim is to precisely define and classify expressions, guarding against misunderstandings about what qualifies as compound and how statements are defined.

Key Takeaway
A complete compound is self-contained; khabar is defined by its essential capacity for truth and falsehood, not by the actual truth-value of particular instances.

Understanding Mufrad (Single Word)
Definition of mufrad as the opposite of compound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an expression is mufrad (single) or compound by checking if any part of it is intended to indicate a part of its meaning.

Core Idea
Mufrad is defined by the absence of part-whole meaning indication.

In the logical analysis of language, expressions are divided into two types: compound and single. A compound expression has parts that each contribute to the overall meaning, like 'in the house'. A single expression (mufrad) has no such part-whole relation. The commentator clarifies that the condition is intentional: it is not intended that any part indicates a part of the meaning. This seemingly simple definition is crucial for identifying the basic units of logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Recall the division
The author first says 'Otherwise it is single (mufrad)'. This refers back to the previous definition of compound: an expression whose part indicates a part of its meaning. So mufrad is the opposite.
2
Understand the condition
The commentator explains: 'if it is not intended that part of it indicates a part of its meaning'. This is a negative condition. For a word like 'stone' (hajar), none of the letters h, j, r individually mean anything related to 'stone'. Therefore, it is mufrad.
3
Test with examples
Consider 'man' (rajul). No part indicates a part of 'man'. But 'the man' (al-rajul) has the definite article 'al' which adds definiteness, so it is compound because a part ('al') contributes to meaning.
In Arabic, 'al' is a particle attached to the noun. In logic, it is considered a separate part even if written together.
4
Apply to a phrase
A phrase like 'the book of Zayd' (kitabu zaydin) has parts: 'kitab' and 'zayd' each contribute meaning, and the possessive construction adds relation. Therefore it is compound, not mufrad.

وإلا فمفرد، وهو إن استقل فمع الدلالة بهيئته على أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة كلمة، نحو: في الدار وخمسة عشر قوله وإلا فمفرد: أي وإن لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه. قوله: وهو إن استقل: في الدلالة على معناه بأن لا يحتاج فيها إلى ضم ضميمة. قوله: بهيئته بأن يكون بحيث كلما تحققت هيئته التركيبية في مادة موضوعة متصرفة فيها، فهم واحد الأزمنة الثلاثة، مثلا هيئة "نصر"، وهي المشتملة على ثلاثة حروف مفتوحة متوالية، كلما تحققت فهم الزمان الماضي، لكن بشرط أن يكون تحققها في ضمن مادة موضوعة متصرفة فيها، فلا يرد النقض بنحو جسق وحجر :قوله كلمة في عرف المنطقيين
Otherwise it is single (mufrad). If it is independent, then with indication by its form of one of the three times, it is a word (kalima), such as 'in the house' and 'fifteen'. His saying: 'Otherwise it is single': i.e., if it is not intended that part of it indicates a part of its meaning. His saying: 'If it is independent': in indicating its meaning, such that it does not need the addition of anything else. His saying: 'by its form': in such a way that whenever its compositional form occurs in a subject matter that is a derived word, the understanding of one of the three times occurs, for example the form of 'nasara', which consists of three successive open letters; whenever it occurs, the past time is understood, but on condition that its occurrence is within a subject matter that is a derived word. Thus the objection with examples like 'jasq' and 'hajar' is refuted. His saying: 'word (kalima) according to the logicians'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the single expression (mufrad) and then subdivides it into the word (kalima) which is a verb. First, the commentator explains that 'otherwise it is single' means that if no part of the expression is intended to indicate a part of its meaning (i.e., it is not a compound), then it is mufrad. Then he explains the subdivision: if the mufrad is independent (does not need something else to convey its meaning) and indicates one of the three times (past, present, future) by its form (hay'a), then it is called kalima (verb) in the terminology of logicians. The form is the pattern of letters (such as the past tense pattern 'fa'ala'). The condition is that the form must occur in a subject matter (madda) that is a derived word (mutasarrif), i.e., a verb that can be conjugated. The commentator gives the example of the form of 'nasara' (he helped) which, whenever it occurs in a derived verb, indicates past time. This condition excludes words like 'jasq' (a non-Arabic word) which is not a subject matter, and 'hajar' (stone) which is not derived (it is a noun not derived from a verb). Thus, the definition of kalima only applies to verbs that have a form indicating time, not to nouns like 'ams' (yesterday) which indicate time by their essence, not by form.

Key Takeaway

Understanding Kalima (Verb) in Logic
Definition of kalima as a verb in logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a mufrad expression qualifies as a kalima (verb) by checking its independence and whether it indicates time through its form (hay'a).

Core Idea
A verb is a mufrad that independently indicates time by its pattern, not by its lexical meaning.

Now that we know what a mufrad is, we can subdivide it. The author gives a special subclass called kalima, which in logical terminology means the verb. A verb is not just any word that refers to time; it must indicate time through its compositional form (hay'a). For example, the word 'stood' (qama) has a form that shows past time. This lesson unpacks the conditions and shows why words like 'yesterday' (ams) are not verbs in the logical sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'kalima' in the context of logic. It is a mufrad that meets two additional conditions: independence and indication of time by form.
Kalima here does not mean any word (as in grammar) but specifically the verb.
2
Check independence
The mufrad must be independent, meaning it does not need another word to complete its meaning. A verb like 'stood' (qama) gives a complete sense without a particle, but a preposition like 'in' (fi) needs a noun to complete its meaning.
3
Check indication of time by form
The verb must indicate one of the three times (past, present, future) through its pattern (hay'a). For example, the pattern 'fa'ala' (فتح, نصر) indicates past time. This is different from a word like 'yesterday' (ams) which indicates past time by its meaning, not by its form.
The condition is that whenever the pattern occurs in a derived verb (mutasarrif), it conveys the time. This excludes nouns that happen to have the same pattern but are not derived verbs, like 'hajar' (stone) which has the pattern fa'al but is not a verb.
4
Apply to examples from the passage
The form 'nasara' has the pattern of three open letters, indicating past time. It is a derived verb, so it is a kalima. 'Hajar' (stone) has the same pattern but is not a derived verb, so it is not a kalima. 'Jasq' is a non-Arabic word not in a subject matter, so it fails.

وإلا فمفرد، وهو إن استقل فمع الدلالة بهيئته على أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة كلمة، نحو: في الدار وخمسة عشر قوله وإلا فمفرد: أي وإن لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه. قوله: وهو إن استقل: في الدلالة على معناه بأن لا يحتاج فيها إلى ضم ضميمة. قوله: بهيئته بأن يكون بحيث كلما تحققت هيئته التركيبية في مادة موضوعة متصرفة فيها، فهم واحد الأزمنة الثلاثة، مثلا هيئة "نصر"، وهي المشتملة على ثلاثة حروف مفتوحة متوالية، كلما تحققت فهم الزمان الماضي، لكن بشرط أن يكون تحققها في ضمن مادة موضوعة متصرفة فيها، فلا يرد النقض بنحو جسق وحجر :قوله كلمة في عرف المنطقيين
Otherwise it is single (mufrad). If it is independent, then with indication by its form of one of the three times, it is a word (kalima), such as 'in the house' and 'fifteen'. His saying: 'Otherwise it is single': i.e., if it is not intended that part of it indicates a part of its meaning. His saying: 'If it is independent': in indicating its meaning, such that it does not need the addition of anything else. His saying: 'by its form': in such a way that whenever its compositional form occurs in a subject matter that is a derived word, the understanding of one of the three times occurs, for example the form of 'nasara', which consists of three successive open letters; whenever it occurs, the past time is understood, but on condition that its occurrence is within a subject matter that is a derived word. Thus the objection with examples like 'jasq' and 'hajar' is refuted. His saying: 'word (kalima) according to the logicians'
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the single expression (mufrad) and then subdivides it into the word (kalima) which is a verb. First, the commentator explains that 'otherwise it is single' means that if no part of the expression is intended to indicate a part of its meaning (i.e., it is not a compound), then it is mufrad. Then he explains the subdivision: if the mufrad is independent (does not need something else to convey its meaning) and indicates one of the three times (past, present, future) by its form (hay'a), then it is called kalima (verb) in the terminology of logicians. The form is the pattern of letters (such as the past tense pattern 'fa'ala'). The condition is that the form must occur in a subject matter (madda) that is a derived word (mutasarrif), i.e., a verb that can be conjugated. The commentator gives the example of the form of 'nasara' (he helped) which, whenever it occurs in a derived verb, indicates past time. This condition excludes words like 'jasq' (a non-Arabic word) which is not a subject matter, and 'hajar' (stone) which is not derived (it is a noun not derived from a verb). Thus, the definition of kalima only applies to verbs that have a form indicating time, not to nouns like 'ams' (yesterday) which indicate time by their essence, not by form.

Equality and Disjointness: The Two Extreme Relations
Author defines equality and disjointness and reduces them to pairs of universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify co-extensiveness (tasāwin) and disjointness (tabāyun) between two universals and state their propositional references as pairs of universal propositions.

Core Idea
Equality and disjointness are the two extreme relations, captured by pairs of universal affirmatives or universal negatives.

In this lesson, we explore the first two of the four relations between universals: equality (tasāwin) and disjointness (tabāyun). These are the simplest relations, where either all instances of both universals coincide (equality) or none do (disjointness). The author then shows how each reduces to two categorical propositions, which will be essential for later syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relation
Check whether the universal truth (ṣidq kullī) holds from both sides (equality) or from neither side (disjointness). For equality, every instance of A is B and every instance of B is A. For disjointness, no instance of A is B and no instance of B is A.
2
Extract the propositional form
For equality, write two universal affirmatives: 'Every A is B' and 'Every B is A'. For disjointness, write two universal negatives: 'No A is B' and 'No B is A'.
The order of the two propositions does not matter.
3
Apply with Islamic examples
Practice with the examples given: human and rational (equality), human and stone (disjointness). Also think of other pairs from Islamic sciences.
Use actual reality (fi nafs al-amr) not mere mental supposition.

Statement of the four relations. As for the second, then either there is universal truth from both sides or from one side. On the first, they are co-extensive like 'human' and 'rational animal'. On the second, they are absolutely general and specific, like 'animal' and 'human'. The reference of equality is to two universal affirmative propositions, such as 'every human is a rational animal' and 'every rational animal is a human'. The reference of disjointness is to two universal negative propositions, such as 'no human is a stone' and 'no stone is a human'. The reference of absolute generality and specificity is to a universal affirmative proposition whose subject is the specific and predicate is the general, and a particular negative proposition whose subject is the general and predicate is the specific, such as 'every human is an animal' and 'some animal is not human'. The reference of generality and specificity from one aspect is to a particular affirmative proposition and two particular negative propositions, such as 'some animal is white', 'some animal is not white', and 'some white is not animal'.

This passage defines the four relations between universals (co-extensiveness, disjointness, absolute generality/specificity, and generality/specificity from one aspect) and explains how each reduces to a specific pattern of categorical propositions. Then for each relation, he gives the corresponding propositional form: equality corresponds to two universal affirmatives, disjointness to two universal negatives, absolute generality/specificity to a universal affirmative and a particular negative, and generality/specificity from one aspect to a particular affirmative and two particular negatives. Examples are drawn from familiar concepts like human, animal, stone, white, demonstrating the logical relationships via terms common in Islamic discourse (human, animal, stone). The relations are defined in terms of actual truth (al-sidq fi nafs al-amr), not mere mental supposition. The author's approach is systematic: he first establishes the two basic cases (both sides or one side), then for each relation he provides a clear propositional pattern that serves as its 'reference' (marja'), i.e., the minimal set of propositions that accurately capture the logical relationship. Understanding this reduction is foundational for later work in syllogistics and definition.
