Absolute Generality and Generality from One Aspect
Author defines absolute generality and generality from one aspect, then reduces each to a set of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute generality/specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq) and generality/specificity from one aspect (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), and write their propositional references.

Core Idea
Absolute generality is one-way inclusion; partial overlap requires a different propositional pattern.

We now turn to the two remaining relations: absolute generality/specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq) and generality/specificity from one aspect (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh). The author explains that the former is a relation where one universal entirely contains the other, while the latter is a partial overlap. Each has a distinct set of propositions that serve as its logical reference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relation
Check if one universal contains all instances of the other but not vice versa (mutlaq) or if they overlap partially with each having exclusive instances (min wajh).
In mutlaq, the specific is entirely included in the general; in min wajh, neither is fully contained in the other.
2
Extract the propositional form for mutlaq
Write a universal affirmative with the specific as subject and the general as predicate, plus a particular negative with the general as subject and the specific as predicate.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is not human'.
3
Extract the propositional form for min wajh
Write one particular affirmative (showing overlap) and two particular negatives (showing that each has instances not in the other).
Example: 'Some animal is white', 'Some animal is not white', 'Some white is not animal'.
4
Apply with Islamic examples
Think of pairs from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'al-mu'min' (believer) and 'al-muslim' (muslim) in actual reality: all believers are Muslims but not all Muslims are believers? Actually that's mutlaq. For min wajh, e.g., 'al-ṣā'im' (fasting) and 'al-musāfir' (traveling).
Ensure the examples are based on actual reality, not mental possibility.

The Four Relations Between Universal Terms
Defining the four relations with their propositional indicators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four relations between universal terms and identify the categorical proposition forms that characterize each.

Core Idea
The four relations map directly to patterns of agreement and difference in extension.

When two universal terms are compared, their relationship falls into one of four categories: equality, disparity, absolute general-specific, or general-specific from one aspect. Each relation corresponds to a specific set of categorical propositions. Understanding these relations is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify equality (tasawi)
Two terms are equal if every individual of one is an individual of the other and vice versa. This is represented by two universal affirmative propositions, e.g., 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is human'.
Terms with identical extension are interchangeable in definitions.
2
Identify disparity (tabayun)
Two terms are disparate if they share no individuals. This is shown by two universal negative propositions, e.g., 'No human is a stone' and 'No stone is a human'.
Disparity means the terms are mutually exclusive categories.
3
Identify absolute general-specific (umum wa khususi mutlaq)
One term includes all individuals of the other and more. This is represented by a universal affirmative (subject = specific term, predicate = general term) and a particular negative (subject = general term, predicate = specific term), e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is not human'.
The specific term is narrower in extension.
4
Identify general-specific from one aspect (umum wa khususi min wajh)
Two terms overlap partially, each having individuals not in the other. This is represented by a particular affirmative and two particular negatives, e.g., 'Some animal is white', 'Some animal is not white', 'Some white is not animal'.
The overlap is partial; neither term completely includes the other.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Explanation of the four relations. And their two contradictories are likewise, or from one side general and specific absolutely. To two universal affirmative (propositions) like: every human is rational and every rational is human; and the reference of disparity to two universal negatives like: no human is a stone and no stone is a human; and the reference of absolute general and specific to a universal affirmative whose subject is the more specific and predicate the more general, and a particular negative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific like: every human is an animal and some animal is not human; and the reference of general and specific from one aspect to a particular affirmative and two particular negatives like: some animal is white and some animal is not white and some white is not animal. His saying: 'and their two contradictories are likewise' means that the two contradictories of two equal (terms) are equal... to two universal affirmatives because the predication of a universal like 'human' on all individuals of another universal like 'rational' is a universal affirmative, and the predication of this other on all individuals of that universal is another universal affirmative. The universal affirmative is the material of agreement; as for the particular negative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific, it is the material of difference; thus in absolute general and specific there are two materials: the material of difference and the material of agreement. (Isma'il: 'particular negative': and he did not say to a particular affirmative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific because there is no order for an affirmative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific, but to two particular affirmatives because the predication of this universal on individuals of that universal partially is a particular affirmative, and the predication of that universal on individuals of this universal partially is another particular affirmative, and he did not say that because the particular affirmative, since it does not convert except as a particular affirmative, it is necessary; because the contrapositive of its contradictory is necessary for it, unlike the particular negative which has no conversion.) (Abd: 'particular affirmative': it is the material of agreement; as for the two particular negatives, each is a material of difference, and difference here is from both sides, so for agreement here there is one material and for difference two materials.) (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that defines the four relations (al-nisab al-arba') between universal terms. The author first lists the four relations: equality (tasawi), disparity (tabayun), absolute general and specific (umum wa khususi mutlaq), and general and specific from one aspect (umum wa khususi min wajh). For each relation, he gives the propositional forms that represent the logical relationship: equality is shown by two universal affirmatives (e.g., 'every human is rational' and 'every rational is human'), disparity by two universal negatives, absolute general and specific by a universal affirmative (subject: specific, predicate: general) and a particular negative (subject: general, predicate: specific), and general and specific from one aspect by a particular affirmative and two particular negatives. Then the commentator explains the statement in the matn: 'their two contradictories are likewise', meaning if two terms are equal, their contradictories are also equal. He provides the proof: if terms are equal (like human and rational), their contradictories (non-human and non-rational) are also equal because the predication of one on the other is mutual. The proof involves the conversion of propositions and the principle that the particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, while the particular negative does not convert. The marginal notes by Isma'il and Abd further clarify why the matn says 'two universal affirmatives' for equality and not something else.

تباين
عموم وخصوص مطلق

The Contradictories of Equal Terms
Explaining and proving the equality of contradictories of equal terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that if two universal terms are equal, their contradictories are also equal using the principle of contraposition.

Core Idea
Equality between terms implies equality between their contradictories a key logical transformation.

The matn states that the contradictories of two equal terms are likewise equal. The commentator expands on this, showing that equality is represented by two universal affirmatives, and by applying contraposition (conversion by negation), the equality of the contradictories follows. This principle is fundamental for transforming terms in logical and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim
The claim is: If term A and term B are equal (identical extension), then the contradictories non-A and non-B are also equal. The matn says 'and their two contradictories are likewise'.
The contradictories here are term-level negations (e.g., non-human).
2
Represent equality propositionally
Equality between A and B means 'Every A is B' and 'Every B is A' are both true. These are two universal affirmatives.
This is the definition of equality from the first lesson.
3
Apply contraposition
From 'Every A is B' we deduce 'Every non-B is non-A' (contrapositive). From 'Every B is A' we deduce 'Every non-A is non-B'. These two universal affirmatives show that non-A and non-B are also equal.
Contraposition is valid for universal affirmatives but not for particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان النسب الأربعة
و نقيضاهما كذلك، أو من جانب عام وأخص مطلقا
·
إلى موجبتين كليتين نحو كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق إنسان، ومرجع التباين إلى سالبتين كليتين، نحو: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر ولا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، ومرجع العموم والخصوص مطلقا إلى موجبة كلية موضوعها الأخص ومحمولها الأعم، وسالبة جزئية موضوعها الأعم ومحمولها الأخص نحو كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، ومرجع العموم والخصوص من وجه إلى موجبة جزئية وسالبتين جزئيتين، نحو: بعض الحيوان أبيض وبعض الحيوان ليس بأبيض وبعض الأبيض ليس بحيوان. قوله ونقيضاهما كذلك يعني إن نقيضي المتساويين ...... موجبتين كليتين لأن صدق الكلي كالإنسان على جميع أفراد كلي آخر كالناطق موجبة كلية وصدق هذا الآخر على جميع أفراد ذلك الكلي موجبة كلية أخرى. موجبة كلية هي مادة التصادق، وأما السالبة الجزئية التي موضوعها الأعم ومحمولها الأخص فهي مادة التفارق، ففي العموم والخصوص مطلقا مادتان مادة التفارق ومادة الاجتماع. (إسماعيل سالبة جزئية: ولم يقل إلى موجبة جزئية موضوعها الأعم ومحمولها الأخص؛ لأنها لا رتبة لموجبة موضوعها الأعم ومحمولها الأخص. موجبة جزئية بل إلى موجبتين جزئيتين؛ لأن صدق هذا الكلي على أفراد ذلك الكلي جزئيا موجبة جزئية وصدق ذلك الكلي على أفراد هذا الكلي جزئيا موجبة جزئية أخرى، ولم يقل ذلك؛ لأن الموجبة الجزئية لما لم تنعكس إلا موجبة جزئية، فيلزم ذلك؛ لأن عكس نقضيه لازم لها بخلاف السالبة الجزئية فإنه لا عكس لها . (عبد) موجبة جزئية: هي مادة الاجتماع، وأما السالبتان الجزئيتان فكل منهما مادة التفارق، والتفارق ههنا من الجانبين، فللتصادق ههنا مادة واحدة وللتفارق مادتان . (إسماعيل
Explanation of the four relations. And their two contradictories are likewise, or from one side general and specific absolutely. To two universal affirmative (propositions) like: every human is rational and every rational is human; and the reference of disparity to two universal negatives like: no human is a stone and no stone is a human; and the reference of absolute general and specific to a universal affirmative whose subject is the more specific and predicate the more general, and a particular negative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific like: every human is an animal and some animal is not human; and the reference of general and specific from one aspect to a particular affirmative and two particular negatives like: some animal is white and some animal is not white and some white is not animal. His saying: 'and their two contradictories are likewise' means that the two contradictories of two equal (terms) are equal... to two universal affirmatives because the predication of a universal like 'human' on all individuals of another universal like 'rational' is a universal affirmative, and the predication of this other on all individuals of that universal is another universal affirmative. The universal affirmative is the material of agreement; as for the particular negative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific, it is the material of difference; thus in absolute general and specific there are two materials: the material of difference and the material of agreement. (Isma'il: 'particular negative': and he did not say to a particular affirmative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific because there is no order for an affirmative whose subject is the more general and predicate the more specific, but to two particular affirmatives because the predication of this universal on individuals of that universal partially is a particular affirmative, and the predication of that universal on individuals of this universal partially is another particular affirmative, and he did not say that because the particular affirmative, since it does not convert except as a particular affirmative, it is necessary; because the contrapositive of its contradictory is necessary for it, unlike the particular negative which has no conversion.) (Abd: 'particular affirmative': it is the material of agreement; as for the two particular negatives, each is a material of difference, and difference here is from both sides, so for agreement here there is one material and for difference two materials.) (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that defines the four relations (al-nisab al-arba') between universal terms. The author first lists the four relations: equality (tasawi), disparity (tabayun), absolute general and specific (umum wa khususi mutlaq), and general and specific from one aspect (umum wa khususi min wajh). For each relation, he gives the propositional forms that represent the logical relationship: equality is shown by two universal affirmatives (e.g., 'every human is rational' and 'every rational is human'), disparity by two universal negatives, absolute general and specific by a universal affirmative (subject: specific, predicate: general) and a particular negative (subject: general, predicate: specific), and general and specific from one aspect by a particular affirmative and two particular negatives. Then the commentator explains the statement in the matn: 'their two contradictories are likewise', meaning if two terms are equal, their contradictories are also equal. He provides the proof: if terms are equal (like human and rational), their contradictories (non-human and non-rational) are also equal because the predication of one on the other is mutual. The proof involves the conversion of propositions and the principle that the particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, while the particular negative does not convert. The marginal notes by Isma'il and Abd further clarify why the matn says 'two universal affirmatives' for equality and not something else.

Key Terms
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the propositional form of equality between two terms A and B?
A) Every A is B and some B is not A.
B) No A is B and no B is A.
C) Every A is B and every B is A.
D) Some A is B and some A is not B.

Key Takeaway
Equality between terms is preserved under contraposition, so the contradictories of equal terms are also equal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given that the terms 'human' (insan) and 'rational' (natiq) are equal, write the two universal affirmatives and then derive the two universal affirmatives that show 'non-human' and 'non-rational' are equal.
?
In a debate, your opponent claims that since 'every literate person is a human' and 'every human is literate' (false), then the contradictories are also equal. Identify the mistake using the requirement of genuine equality.

Contradictories of Absolute General and Specific are Reversed
Author proves the reversal of contradictories with a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and prove the reversal relationship between the contradictories of absolute general and specific terms using the laws of contradiction.

Core Idea
The contradictory of the general is specific; the contradictory of the specific is general.

How do the contradictories of general and specific terms relate? This passage from the Sharh al-Tahdhib demonstrates a key logical insight: the relationship reverses. Understanding this helps in analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences where terms and their negations are used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author states that the contradictories of absolute general and specific are reversed: the contradictory of the general is specific and vice versa.
2
Understand the proof structure
The proof uses the impossibility of both contradictories being false (rise) and both being true (conjunction) to show that predication of one contradictory entails predication of the other's contradictory.
This is a proof by contradiction.
3
Apply the example
Consider 'animal' (general) and 'human' (specific). Their contradictories are 'non-animal' and 'non-human'. Show that 'non-animal' is more specific than 'non-human'.
This illustrates the reversal.
4
Examine the objection
The commentator addresses a potential confusion about the impossibility of the rise of contradictories and clarifies the meaning.
This shows subtlety in logical principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقيضاهما بالعكس، أيضاً متساويان أي كل ما صدق عليه أحد النقيضين صدق عليه نقيض الآخر؛ إذ لو صدق أحدهما بدون الآخر لصدق مع عين الآخر؛ ضرورة استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين فيصدق عين الآخر بدون عين الأول؛ لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين وهذا يرفع التساوي بين العينين، مثلا: لو صدق اللا إنسان على شيء و لم يصدق عليه اللاناطق لصدق عليه الناطق فيصدق الناطق ههنا بدون الإنسان هذا خلف قوله ونقيضاهما بالعكس أي نقيض الأعم والأخص مطلقا أعم وأخص مطلقا لكن بعكس العينين فنقيض الأعم أخص، ونقيض الأخص أعم، يعني كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص، وليس كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص صدق عليه نقيض الأعم. الصدق ارتفاع النقيضين يعني أو لم يصدق مع عين الآخر أيضاً كما أنه لا يصدق مع نقيضه لزم ارتفاع النقيضين. فإن قلت إن ارتفاع النقيضين نقيض للنقيضين مع أنهما مرتفعان من كل شيء؛ إذ استحالتهما يمنع من على شيء في نفس الأمر، فارتفاع النقيضين ثابت في نفس الأمر وليس بمحال قلت مراد كم من النقيضين ما ذا أما النقيضان على سبيل الاجتماع فحينئذٍ لا نسلم أن ارتفاع النقيضين بهذا المعنى محال، ولا ندعي بطلانه، إنما المحال هو ارتفاع النقيضين بمعنى ارتفاع كل واحد وأما النقيضان على الترديد فنختار أن ارتفاع النقيضين بهذا المعنى مما ندعي استحالته لكن لا نسلم أن النقيضين بهذا المعنى باطل؛ فإن الباطل هو اجتماع النقيضين. فافهم (إسماعيل لامتناع أي لو صدق عين الآخر مع عين الأول لزم اجتماع النقيضين، وهو ممتنع. صدق اللاإنسان ليس بلا ناطق يستلزم صدق اللا إنسان ناطق. فيه نظر؛ فإن القضية الأولى سالبة معدولة والثانية موجبة محصلة وقد تقرر في موضعه أن السالبة المعدولة أعم من الموجبة المحصلة منها فكيف تستلزمها؟ والجواب بأن الأولى وإن كانت أعم من الثانية إلا أنهما متساويان عند وجود الموضوع وههنا كذلك الموجود اللا إنسان كالفرس. أخص كالحيوان مثلا أخص ونقيض الأخص كاللا إنسان أعم، فكل ما صدق عليه اللاحيوان كالحجر صدق عليه اللاإنسان، وليس كل ما صدق عليه اللاإنسان كالفرس صدق عليه اللاحيوان. (برهان) لصدق يعني
And their two contradictories are opposite, also equal, meaning: everything that one of the two contradictories is predicated of, the contradictory of the other is predicated of it; because if one is predicated without the other, it would be predicated together with the other one, due to the impossibility of the rise of the two contradictories (both false), so the other one would be predicated without the first, due to the impossibility of their conjunction, and this removes the equality between the two terms. For example: if 'non-human' is predicated of something and 'non-rational' is not predicated of it, then 'rational' is predicated of it; thus 'rational' is predicated here without 'human' — this is contrary to his statement. 'And their two contradictories are opposite' means: the contradictory of the absolute general and the absolute specific are absolute general and absolute specific but reversed in terms of the two terms. So the contradictory of the general is specific, and the contradictory of the specific is general. That is: everything that the contradictory of the general is predicated of, the contradictory of the specific is predicated of it, but not everything that the contradictory of the specific is predicated of, the contradictory of the general is predicated of it. (Another reading: 'the truth is the rise of the two contradictories' means: or if it is not predicated together with the other, just as it is not predicated with its contradictory, the rise of the two contradictories follows.) If you say: 'The rise of the two contradictories is a contradictory to the two contradictories, yet they are raised from everything because their impossibility prevents (predication?) on something in actuality, so the rise of the two contradictories is established in actuality and is not impossible', I say: 'What do you mean by the two contradictories? If they are considered as a conjunction, then we do not agree that the rise of the two contradictories in this sense is impossible, nor do we claim its falsity; only the rise of the two contradictories in the sense of the rise of each one is impossible. If they are considered as a disjunction, then we choose that the rise of the two contradictories in this sense is something we claim impossible, but we do not agree that the two contradictories in this sense are false; for the false is the conjunction of the two contradictories.' So understand. (Isma'il: 'due to impossibility': meaning if the other is predicated together with the first, it necessitates the conjunction of the two contradictories, which is impossible. The predication of 'non-human' without 'non-rational' necessitates the predication of 'non-human' and 'rational'. There is a consideration: for the first proposition is a negative with a modified subject and the second is a simple affirmative, and it has been established in its place that the negative with modified subject is more general than the simple affirmative; how then does it necessitate it? The answer: even though the first is more general than the second, they are equal when the subject exists, and here similarly the existing non-human is like a horse. More specific: like 'animal' for example is more specific, and its contradictory 'non-animal' is more general, so everything that 'non-animal' is predicated of, like a stone, 'non-human' is predicated of it, but not everything that 'non-human' is predicated of, like a horse, 'non-animal' is predicated of it.) (Proof:) 'For truth' means...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's statement: 'and their two contradictories are opposite', referring to the contradictories of absolute general and specific terms. The commentator proves that the contradictories are also equal in a reversed sense: the contradictory of the absolute general is the absolute specific, and the contradictory of the absolute specific is the absolute general. For example, if 'human' and 'animal' are in a relation of absolute general and specific ('animal' is general, 'human' is specific), then their contradictories 'non-animal' and 'non-human' are also in a relation of absolute general and specific, but reversed: 'non-animal' is more general than 'non-human'. The commentator provides a proof using the impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories and the impossibility of their rise (both false). He then discusses a potential objection: if the rise of the two contradictories is impossible, how can it be that the rise of contradictories (both false) is sometimes considered? The answer distinguishes two senses: conjunction (both true) which is impossible, and disjunction (each alone) where the rise (both false) is impossible, but the conjunction is also impossible. The marginal note by Isma'il further clarifies an example: if 'non-human' is predicated of something (like a horse) without 'non-rational', then 'rational' must be predicated, leading to 'rational' without 'human'. The note also addresses a subtlety about the conversion of negative modified propositions.

The Impossibility of the Rise of Contradictories: Objection and Clarification
Author addresses an objection about the impossibility of the rise of contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two senses of 'rise of contradictories' and evaluate the objection raised against the principle.

Core Idea
The rise of both contradictories (both false) is impossible only when considered as a disjunction, not as a conjunction.

In the proof of the reversal relationship, an objection is raised: if the rise of contradictories is impossible, how can it be that the statement 'the rise of the two contradictories is a contradictory to the two contradictories' is true? The commentator clarifies the subtlety by distinguishing between two meanings, showing that the apparent contradiction dissolves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that the rise of contradictories is itself a contradictory to the two contradictories, yet it seems to be a true statement, implying that the rise is possible.
2
Understand the commentator's distinction
The commentator distinguishes between two senses of 'the two contradictories': as a conjunction (both true) and as a disjunction (either true). The rise (both false) is impossible only in the disjunctional sense, but the conjunction of the two contradictories is also impossible.
This clarifies the scope of the impossibility.
3
Apply the distinction to the objection
When the objector says 'the rise of the two contradictories is a contradictory to the two contradictories', this refers to the conjunctional sense of 'the two contradictories', so the rise is not a contradictory to that conjunction; rather, the conjunction itself is impossible.
4
Reflect on the marginal note
Isma'il's note addresses a subtlety about negative modified propositions, showing that the entailment in the proof holds when the subject exists, which ties back to the logical principles used.
This deepens the understanding of the original proof.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) When 'the two contradictories' means their conjunction.
B) When 'the two contradictories' means their disjunction.
C) In both senses.
D) In neither sense.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of the rise of contradictories applies to the disjunctional sense, not the conjunctional sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a short dialogue between a student and teacher clarifying the difference between the conjunction and disjunction of contradictories, using the laws of logic from this passage.

Understanding Partial Distinction Between Contradictories of Mutually Exclusive Terms
Explanation of partial distinction between contradictories of mutually exclusive terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the partial distinction between the contradictories of two mutually exclusive universals, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The contradictories of mutually exclusive terms are not mutually exclusive; they share a partial overlap.

This passage explores a subtle but important relation between logical terms: the contradictories of two completely separate (mutually exclusive) universals turn out to have a partial overlap. This refines our understanding of the four logical relations and is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two original universals
Find the two main terms said to be mutually exclusive (mutabayinan). Often these are like 'human' and 'stone' or 'existent' and 'non-existent'.
2
Determine their contradictories
Form the contradictories by negation, e.g., 'non-human' and 'non-stone'. The passage calls these 'naqidayn'.
3
Check the relation between the contradictories
Observe that the contradictories are not mutually exclusive: they can co-apply in some cases (e.g., a tree is both non-human and non-stone) but diverge in others (e.g., a stone is non-human but not non-stone). This is the partial distinction.
4
Contrast with total distinction
When the originals are total opposites like existent and non-existent, their contradictories also have total distinction (non-existent and non-non-existent are equally exclusive).
5
Understand the logical mechanism
The passage proves: if originals are mutually exclusive, each applies with the contradictory of the other, leading each contradictory to apply without the other in some cases. This is what partial distinction means.
The proof relies on the impossibility of both originals applying together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بيان النسب الأربعة
أي كما أن بين نقيضي الأعم والأخص من وجه مباينةً جزئية، كذلك بين نقيضي المتباينين تباين جزئي؛ فإنه لما صدق كل من العينين مع نقيض الآخر صدق كل من النقيضين مع عين الآخر، فصدق كل من النقيضين بدون الآخر في الجملة، وهو التباين الجزئي، ثم إنه قد يتحقق في ضمن التباين الكلي، كالموجود والمعدوم؛ فإن بين نقيضيهما وهما اللاموجود واللامعدوم أيضا تباينا كليا، وقد يتحقق في ضمن العموم من وجه كالإنسان والحجر؛ فإن بين نقيضيهما -وهما اللاإنسان واللاحجر - عموما من وجه فلذا قالوا إن بين نقيضيهما مباينة جزئية حتى يصح في الكل.
هذا واعلم أيضًا أن المصنف أخر ذكر نقيضي المتباينين بوجهين: الأول: قصداً للاختصار

Concepts. Explanation of the four relations. Meaning: Just as there is a partial distinction between the two contradictories of the more general and the more specific from one perspective, similarly there is a partial distinction between the two contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things. Because when each of the two things applies with the contradictory of the other, each of the two contradictories applies with the other thing, so each of the two contradictories applies without the other in some respect, which is the partial distinction. Then it may be realized within the total distinction, like the existent and the non-existent; for between their two contradictories, which are the non-existent and the non-non-existent, there is also total distinction. And it may be realized within the generality from one perspective, like human and stone; for between their two contradictories, which are non-human and non-stone, there is generality from one perspective. Hence they said that between their two contradictories there is partial distinction so that it holds in all. This, and know also that the author delayed mentioning the two contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things for two reasons: first, for brevity. Meaning: as you know, the author's expression requires that the contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things be the similized and the contradictories of the more general and more specific from one perspective be the similizing, and the commentator's expression requires that the contradictories of the more general and more specific be the similized to them and the contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things be the similizing. Possibly the reason is that if the contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things were the similized as understood from the apparent meaning of his expression, it would necessitate that his mention be before the similized, which is not the case. Hence the commentator made it opposite. Partial distinction. And the secret in that is that the two things do not apply one with the other, like human because it does not apply with stone, and similarly stone does not apply with human. So each applies with the contradictory of the other. For example, human, since it does not apply with stone, must apply with the contradictory of stone, which is non-stone, otherwise it would necessitate the elevation of the two contradictories. Similarly, stone, since it does not apply with human, inevitably applies with non-human, due to the impossibility of the elevation of the two contradictories. And when each of the two mutually exclusive things applies with the contradictory of the other, each of them does not apply with the other thing. And when each of the two contradictories applies with the other thing, then each of the two contradictories applies without the other, and that state is the partial distinction. Total distinction: the separation between two universals in all materials, and within it the separation in some materials is also realized, which is the partial distinction. 'Distinction': for the non-existent is in the power of the non-existing and the non-non-existent is in the power of the existent, so it is impossible for each to apply to the other, otherwise it would necessitate a single thing being both existent and non-existent, which is impossible. Ismail: Generality from one perspective because they combine in tree and separate in this stone and Zayd. (Tuhfa: This is an object of an omitted verb whose agent is implicit, meaning 'Take this'. This is the well-known view. It has been said that 'ha' is a verb meaning 'take' and 'dha' is its object in the accusative case, so this, although it violates orthography, yet it has safety from ellipsis. Ismail) And know the answer to what is said: Why did he not mention the relation between the two contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things immediately after them?
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the partial distinction (al-tabaayun al-juz'i) between the contradictories of two terms that are mutually exclusive (mutabayinayn). The author begins by drawing an analogy: just as there is a partial distinction between the contradictories of terms that have a 'generality from one perspective' relation (like human and white), similarly there is a partial distinction between the contradictories of two mutually exclusive terms (like human and stone). The reason is that each of the two terms applies with the contradictory of the other: e.g., human does not apply with stone, so human must apply with non-stone, and stone with non-human. Consequently, each contradictory applies without the other in some cases, which is the partial distinction. The author then notes that this partial distinction can occur within two broader relations: total distinction (as with existent and non-existent, where the contradictories also have total distinction) or within generality from one perspective (as with human and stone, where non-human and non-stone share some instances like trees and differ in others). Next, the author explains why the original text (the matn) delayed mentioning the contradictories of mutually exclusive terms. Two reasons are given: brevity and the fact that the conception of partial distinction depends on first understanding its two subtypes (total distinction and generality from one perspective). The commentary then delves into the details of the analogy, clarifying that the author of the matn intended the contradictories of mutually exclusive terms to be the subject of comparison, while the contradictories of 'generality from one perspective' are the standard. The secret is that mutually exclusive terms cannot apply together, so each must apply with the contradictory of the other, leading to the partial distinction. Examples from Islamic logic: human and stone are mutually exclusive (a single object cannot be both), and their contradictories non-human and non-stone have generality from one perspective (both apply to a tree, but non-human applies to stones and non-stone to humans). The passage ends with a rhetorical question about why the author did not place the discussion of contradictories immediately after the discussion of the two mutually exclusive terms, implying the answer is the same as the two reasons given.

Two Meanings of 'Partial' (al-Juz'i): Real and Relative
Author defines second meaning of al-juz'i and contrasts with the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real partial (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) and a relative partial (al-juz'i al-idafi) and explain why the relative partial can be a universal in another context.

Core Idea
The term 'partial' means both the individual that cannot be shared and the more specific term in a hierarchy.

The term 'juz'i' in logic has two distinct meanings. The first is the real partial, which is an individual like Zayd that cannot be predicated of many. The second is the relative partial, which is a term that is more specific than another, like 'man' relative to 'animal'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the hierarchy of concepts in the five universals and for constructing precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two meanings
Find the sentence where the author says: 'وقد يقال الجزئي للأخص من الشيء' – meaning 'juz'i' is also said of the more specific. Note that this is a second usage, not replacing the first.
The first meaning is the real partial; the second is the relative partial.
2
Distinguish real vs relative
Identify the terms 'الحقيقي' and 'الإضافي' in the commentary. Real partial (like Zayd) cannot be predicated of many. Relative partial (like man) is more specific than something else (animal) but can be predicated of many (Zayd, Amr).
The relative partial can be a universal when considered alone.
3
Test the relationship
The passage states every real partial is under a general concept (e.g., 'thing'), but not every relative partial is a real partial. Verify with examples: Zayd is under 'man' and 'animal'; but 'man' is not a real partial because it can be predicated.
The objection about a real partial without a general concept is refuted by the concept 'thing' being always applicable.
4
Apply to a new example
Given the term 'horse', determine if it is a real partial or a relative partial relative to 'animal'. Explain that 'horse' is a relative partial (more specific than animal) but a universal relative to individual horses.
Check the criteria: can it be predicated of many? Yes, so it is not a real partial.

أنه

The second application of the partial. And it may be said: the partial is [used] for the more specific of something. By analogy with the contradictory of the more general and more specific from one perspective. And the second: that the conception of the partial distinction, inasmuch as it is stripped of the particularity of its two individuals, depends on the conception of its two individuals which are the generality from one perspective and the total distinction. So before mentioning both of its individuals, its mention is not feasible. His saying: 'and it may be said the partial etc.' means that the term 'partial' is applied to the concept which the mind cannot conceive of applying to many, similarly it is applied to the more specific of something. According to the first, it is qualified by 'real', and according to the second, by 'relative'. And the partial in the second sense is more general than in the first sense; for every real partial is subsumed under a general concept, and the least of that is the concept, the thing, and the matter, and not vice versa; for the relative partial may be a universal like man relative to animal. From it: from where, i.e., considering it being general without the particularity of its two individuals. So the result is that knowledge of the partial distinction in terms of its being general without the total distinction and the generality from one perspective depends on knowledge of them, i.e., it is not clear with full clarity except after they are clear. So understand. (Ismail) Similarly, it is applied: meaning the term 'partial' is shared between the two meanings: the first is what passed, which is that which cannot be supposed to apply to many, like Zayd, and it is opposite to the universal. The second: the more specific of something, i.e., that which is subsumed under the more general, like man. This meaning is not opposite to the universal, rather it may coexist with it, like man; for it is universal with respect to Zayd and Amr and others, and partial with respect to being under animal, and animal is more general. And it may not coexist with it, like Zayd; for he is a relative partial because he is subsumed under the general concept of man, and he is not a universal due to the impossibility of his applying to many. And this is apparent. (Ismail) It is qualified: meaning the partial in the previously mentioned sense is called a real partial; for it is partial with respect to its own essence, because it prevents sharing in the external world. And the partial in the sense mentioned here is called a relative partial; for its partialness is with respect to other than it, which is the general. So much so that if there were nothing more general than it, its partialness would cease. Because the real partial is the person, and it is subsumed under the universal essence stripped of individuations; for it is the essence qualified by individuation, and the stripped essence is the absolute, so it is more general due to its existence in that partial and in other than it. (Abd) And the least of it: repelling an expected objection whose content is: we do not accept that every real partial is under a more general; for it is possible that it is not subsumed under any general thing. And its repelling: by the concept of thing being general. And its repelling: by the concept of the term 'concept', 'thing', 'matter', and similarly 'the general possible' is not empty of them, so the real partial also must enter under them. (Tuhfa)
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the second meaning of the term 'juz'i' in logic. The author begins by stating that 'juz'i' is also applied to the more specific (akhass) of something, not only to the individual that cannot be predicated of many. This is derived by analogy with the contradictory of terms with 'generality from one perspective'. The passage then explains that the conception of partial distinction (tabaayun juz'i) depends on first understanding its two subtypes: generality from one perspective and total distinction. The commentary clarifies that the term 'juz'i' is shared between two meanings: the real partial (juz'i haqiqi), which is an individual like Zayd that cannot be predicated of many, and the relative partial (juz'i idafi), which is a more specific term relative to a more general one, like man relative to animal. The relative partial is broader because every real partial is subsumed under a general concept (at least the concepts of 'concept', 'thing', or 'matter'), but not vice versa: a relative partial like 'man' can be a universal when considered relative to individuals. The passage also addresses an objection: if every real partial must be under a more general concept, what about a hypothetical individual with no general concept? The refutation is that even the concept of 'thing' is general, so no real partial escapes being under some general concept. Examples from Islamic knowledge: Zayd is a real partial (cannot be predicated of many), while 'man' is a relative partial when compared to 'animal' but a universal when compared to Zayd and Amr. This distinction is important for understanding the hierarchy of universals and partials in logic.

Three Types of Lāzim: Quiddity, External Existence, Mental Existence
Commentator divides necessary concomitants by essence/existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of necessary concomitant based on their dependence on quiddity or existence.

Core Idea
A concomitant may be tied to the essence itself or to a specific mode of existence.

In logic, understanding the nature of necessary concomitants is crucial for defining things. The commentator expands on the matn by dividing the necessary concomitant into three types: one tied to the quiddity itself, and two tied to specific modes of existence. This lesson helps you identify these types.

Guided Path
1
Clarify initial classification
Recall that a universal accident is either a necessary concomitant (lāzim) or a separable accident (mufāriq). The lāzim is further divided here.
2
Identify the first division criterion
The first division looks at whether the concomitant is attached to the quiddity (māhiyya) itself, disregarding existence, or to a particular mode of existence (wujūd).
Quiddity is the essence considered independently of existence.
3
Distinguish the three resulting types
From this division we get: (1) lāzim al-māhiyya, which holds regardless of existence (e.g., evenness for four); (2) lāzim al-wujūd al-khārijī, which holds specifically in external existence (e.g., burning for fire); (3) lāzim al-wujūd al-dhihnī, which holds specifically in mental existence (e.g., universality for the concept of man).
4
Recognize the secondary intelligible
Note that the concomitant of mental existence is also called 'secondary intelligible' (ma'qūl thānī).
This term is important in later Islamic logic and metaphysics.

قوله وعلى غيرها كالماشي يقال على حقيقة الإنسان وعلى غيرها من الحقائق الحيوانية. قوله: وكل منهما: أي كل واحد من الخاصة والعرض العام، وبالجملة الكلي الذي هو عرض لأفراده إما لازم أو مفارق؛ إذ لا يخلو إما أن يستحيل انفكاكه عن معروضه أو لا، فالأول هو الأول والثاني هو الثاني، ثم اللازم ينقسم بتقسيمين: أحدهما: أن لازم الشيء إما لازم له بالنظر إلى نفس الماهية مع قطع النظر عن خصوص وجودها في الخارج أو في الذهن؛ وذلك بأن يكون هذا الشيء بحيث كلما تحقق في الذهن أو في الخارج كان هذا اللازم ثابتا له، وإما لازم له بالنظر إلى وجوده أي إلى خصوص وجوده الخارجي أو الذهني. فهذا القسم بالحقيقة قسمان فأقسام اللازم بهذا التقسيم ثلاثة: لازم الماهية كزوجية الأربعة، ولازم الوجود الخارجي كإحراق النار، ولازم الوجود الذهني ككون حقيقة الإنسان كلية، فهذا القسم يسمى معقولا ثانيا أيضاً، والثاني أن اللازم إما بين أو غير بين، والبين له معنيان: أحدهما: الذي يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم كما يلزم تصور البصر من تصور العمى، فهذا ما يقال له بين بالمعنى الأخص وحينئذ فغير البين هو اللازم الذي لا يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم كالكتابة بالقوة للإنسان، والثاني من معنى البين هو اللازم الذي يلزم من تصوره مع تصور الملزوم، والنسبة بينهما الجزم باللزوم وغير بين بخلافه.
His statement: 'and upon others' like 'walker' is said of the reality of man and upon other animal realities. His statement: 'and each of them' means each of the special property and the common accident, and in summary, the universal that is an accident for its individuals is either a necessary concomitant or a separable [accident]; since it is unavoidable that either its detachment from its subject is impossible or not, the first is the first and the second is the second. Then the necessary concomitant is divided by two divisions: One of them: that the concomitant of a thing is either a concomitant of it with respect to the essence itself, disregarding the particularity of its existence in the external or in the mind; such that this thing is such that whenever it is realized in the mind or in the external, this concomitant is established for it; or a concomitant of it with respect to its existence, i.e., with respect to its particular external or mental existence. So this division is in reality two divisions, so the divisions of the concomitant by this division are three: concomitant of quiddity like the evenness of four, concomitant of external existence like the burning of fire, and concomitant of mental existence like the fact that the reality of man is a universal. This division is also called secondary intelligible. The second [division] is that the concomitant is either evident or non-evident, and 'evident' has two meanings: One of them: that of which the conception follows from the conception of the subject, just as the conception of sight follows from the conception of blindness. This is called evident in the most specific sense, and then the non-evident is the concomitant whose conception does not follow from the conception of the subject, like potential writing for man. The second meaning of 'evident' is the concomitant for which the judgment of concomitance follows from its conception together with the conception of the subject, and the relation between them. Non-evident is the opposite of this.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on the division of necessary concomitants (lāzim) as presented in the matn. The commentator first clarifies that a universal which is an accident for its individuals is either a necessary concomitant (lāzim) or a separable accident (mufāriq). The necessary concomitant is then divided in two ways: First, with respect to whether it is tied to the quiddity (māhiyya) itself or to the specific mode of existence (wujūd) of that quiddity. The commentator explains that if the concomitant is bound to the quiddity regardless of whether it exists in mind or externally, it is a concomitant of quiddity, like evenness for the number four. If it is tied specifically to external existence, like fire's burning, it is a concomitant of external existence. If tied specifically to mental existence, like the universality of the concept of man, it is a concomitant of mental existence. The latter is also called a secondary intelligible (ma'qūl thānī). The second division is between evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin) concomitants. 'Evident' has two sub-meanings: (1) evident in the most specific sense, where the concept of the concomitant follows immediately from the concept of the subject, such as the concept of sight following from blindness (a privative relation). (2) Evident in a broader sense, where the judgment of concomitance requires both concepts but no further inference, like knowing that four is even once you conceive of four and evenness together. The non-evident is everything else, requiring external proof. This classification helps in logic to distinguish between definitions and demonstrations, and in theology to differentiate between essential and accidental attributes.

Key Terms
اللازم
الماهية
الوجود
المعقول الثاني

D) The oddness of the number three

Key Takeaway
The necessary concomitant is not uniform; it can depend on the quiddity alone or on a specific mode of existence, yielding three distinct types.

Evident and Non-Evident Concomitants: Two Levels of Clarity
Commentator divides necessary concomitants by evidentness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a necessary concomitant as evident or non-evident and distinguish the two sub-meanings of 'evident'.

Core Idea
An evident concomitant is either immediately known from its subject alone or from combining both concepts.

In this part of the commentary, the scholar introduces another division of necessary concomitants: whether the concomitance is evident (bayyin) or non-evident. He further explains that 'evident' has two levels of immediacy. This lesson will sharpen your ability to judge when a logical connection is self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the second division
Every necessary concomitant is either evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin). The commentator defines two meanings of 'evident'.
2
Understand the first meaning (most specific)
A concomitant is evident in the most specific sense when its conception follows solely from the conception of the subject. Example: from 'blindness', the concept of 'sight' follows (as a privation).
This is a strong immediacy; no extra step is needed.
3
Understand the second meaning (specific)
A concomitant is evident in a broader sense when the judgment of concomitance follows from having both the subject and concomitant concepts together. Example: conceiving of 'four' and 'evenness' together makes you judge that four is even.
This requires both concepts, but no external proof.
4
Contrast with non-evident
A non-evident concomitant requires external proof; its connection is not immediately clear from the concepts alone. Example: 'potential writing' is not evident from the concept of 'man' alone.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Three Parts of a Proposition
Defines and classifies the three parts of a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and copula in any categorical proposition and classify it as three-part or two-part.

Core Idea
A proposition is built from subject, predicate, and a copula that may be hidden.

Understanding the structure of a proposition is the first step in logical analysis. This lesson explains the three essential parts and the difference between explicit and implicit copulas.

Guided Path
1
Locate the subject and predicate
In the proposition, find the entity being spoken about (subject) and what is attributed to it (predicate). Note that in Arabic, the subject often takes raf'.
2
Find the copula
The copula is the word or sign that links subject and predicate. It may be explicit like 'huwa' or implied by the syntax. If explicit, the proposition is three-part; if not, two-part.
3
Classify the proposition
Based on the presence of an explicit copula, label the proposition as thulathiyya or thunaiyya.

قوله : محمولا لأنه أمر جعل محمولا لموضوعه قوله والدال على النسبة: أي اللفظة المذكورة في القضية الملفوظة التي تدل على النسبة الحكمية تسمى ،رابطة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول؛ فإن الرابطة حقيقة هي النسبة ،الحكمية وفي قوله والدال على النسبة" إشارة إلى أن الرابطة أداة؛ لدلالتها على النسبة التي هي معنى حرفي غير مستقل واعلم أن الرابطة قد تذكر في القضية، وقد تحذف فالقضية على الأول تسمى ثلاثيةً وعلى الثاني ثنائية قوله وقد استعير لها "هو": اعلم أن الرابطة تنقسم إلى زمانية تدل على اقتران النسبة الحكمية بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة، وغير زمانية بخلاف ذلك، وذكر الفارابي أن الحكمة الفلسفية لما نقلت من اللغة اليونانية إلى العربية وجد القوم
جعل محمولا وهو قد يكون كلمة، مثل: زيد يقرب، وقد يكون قضية مثل: زيد أبوه قائم، وقد يكون اسما، مثل: كل إنسان حيوان باسم المدلول: الأولى أن يقول بوصف المدلول؛ فإن الرابطة ليست أسماء للنسبة الحكمية، بل إنما هو وصف له إسماعيل النسبة أي على النسبة التي هي مورد الحكم والإذعان؛ لأنه لم يسم اللفظ الدال على نسبة يربط بها الموضوع بالمحمول رابطة ما لم يعتبر معها الوقوع واللاوقوع، ولفظة "هو" رابطة الإيجاب و لم يعتبروا رابطة السلب استغناء بها مع وجود حرف السلب. (عبد) تحذف أي لم تذكر الرابطة في اللفظ اكتفاء عنها بالحركات التي هي علامات دالة عليها بالالتزام لا بالمطابقة كالرفع في الموضوع والمحمول ؛ فإنه دال على كون أحدهما مبتدأ أو محكوما عليه والآخر خبرا ثابتا أو محكوما به، وهذه الدلالة بالالتزام بالمطابقة؛ إذ الإعراب لم يوضع للربط، بل للدلالة على المعاني المعتورة على المعرب، ويلزمها الربط ويفهم منه المعنى الرابطي. (مرآة الشروح للسلم
استعير: اعلم أن الاستعارة لا بدلها من المستعير والمستعار والمستعار منه المستعير والمستعار والمستعار منه والعجز والافتقار، فشرع في بيان كل منها. فالقوم الناقلون هم المستعيرون والمستعار كلمة "هو" و"هي"، والمستعار منه هو الاسم، وعدم وجدانهم رابطة غير زمانية في كلام العرب عند احتياجهم إليها عجز وافتقار . (عبد) وقد استعير لها هو: جواب عما يقال: إن كون الدال على النسبة الرابطة أداة ممنوع بسند أن "هو" في "زيد" هو قائم" يدل على النسبة، وليس بأداة؛ لأنه اسم فأجاب بقوله: "وقد استعير" "لها" للدلالة على النسبة لفظة "هو" مثلا، يعني أن في أصل الوضع اسم.
"هو" ليس بأداة، ثم بالنقل والاستعارة إنه دل على المعنى النسبي التي هي معنى حرفي، فلا ضير. (عبد)
Assents 88. Names of the parts of the proposition: predicate, and the indicator of the relation is the copula, and 'huwa' has been borrowed for it. His statement: 'predicate' because it is something made a predicate for its subject. His statement: 'and the indicator of the relation': that is, the word mentioned in the verbal proposition that indicates the judgmental relation is called a copula, naming the indicator by the name of the indicated; for the copula in reality is the judgmental relation. And in his statement 'and the indicator of the relation' there is an indication that the copula is a tool, because it indicates the relation which is a letter-meaning, not independent. Know that the copula may be mentioned in the proposition, and it may be omitted. So the proposition in the first case is called three-part, and in the second two-part. His statement: 'and 'huwa' has been borrowed for it': know that the copula is divided into temporal, indicating the conjunction of the judgmental relation with one of the three times, and non-temporal, contrary to that. Al-Farabi mentioned that when philosophical wisdom was translated from the Greek language into Arabic, the people found... made predicate, and it may be a word like 'Zayd is approaching', or it may be a proposition like 'Zayd his father is standing', or it may be a noun like 'every human is an animal'. By the name of the indicated: it is better to say 'by the description of the indicated' because the copula is not a name for the judgmental relation, but rather it is a description of it. Ismail: 'the relation' i.e. the relation that is the object of judgment and assent; because the word indicating a relation by which the subject is linked to the predicate is not called a copula unless occurrence and non-occurrence are considered with it. The word 'huwa' is a copula for affirmation, and they did not consider a copula for negation, being independent by the presence of the negation particle. (Abd) 'omitted' i.e. the copula is not mentioned in the wording, being satisfied by the vowel signs that are indicative of it by implication not by correspondence, like the raising (raf') in the subject and predicate; for it indicates that one of them is a mubtada' or subject of judgment, and the other is a predicate established or judged by it. This indication is by implication, not correspondence; because inflection was not placed for linking, but to indicate the meanings that affect the inflected word, and the linking necessarily accompanies it and the relational meaning is understood from it. (Mir'at al-Shuruh lil-Sullam) 'borrowed': know that borrowing necessarily requires a lender, a borrowed thing, and a thing borrowed from; the lenders, the borrowed thing, and the thing borrowed from, and inability and need. So he proceeded to explain each of them. The people who translated are the lenders, the borrowed thing is the word 'huwa' and 'hiya', and the thing borrowed from is the noun. Their not finding a non-temporal copula in the speech of the Arabs when they needed it is inability and need. (Abd) 'And 'huwa' has been borrowed for it': a response to what is said: that the indicator of the relation being a tool is denied based on the fact that 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qa'im' indicates the relation, and it is not a tool because it is a noun. So he answered by saying: 'and 'huwa' has been borrowed for it', i.e., for indicating the relation, the word 'huwa', for example, meaning that in the original position it is a noun. 'Huwa' is not a tool originally, then by transmission and borrowing it came to indicate the relational meaning which is a letter-meaning, so there is no harm. (Abd)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the three parts of a proposition: subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), and copula (rabita). The copula is the term that indicates the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) between subject and predicate. The author explains that the copula may be explicit (making a three-part proposition) or implicit (two-part proposition). For explicit copula, the word 'huwa' is often used, but it is borrowed from its original use as a pronoun. Al-Farabi is cited noting that when Greek philosophy was translated into Arabic, translators borrowed 'huwa' to serve as a copula because Arabic lacked a non-temporal linking word. The copula can be temporal (indicating time) or non-temporal. The passage also clarifies that the copula is a tool (adāt) because it indicates a relational meaning, which is a letter-meaning (ma'na ḥarfī), not an independent concept. When the copula is omitted, the proposition is two-part, and the relational meaning is understood from the inflectional endings (e.g., raf' on subject and predicate). The objection that 'huwa' is a noun not a tool is answered: it is a noun originally but borrowed for the relational meaning, so it functions as a copula without harm.

القضية الثنائية

Key Takeaway
Every categorical proposition consists of subject, predicate, and copula; the copula may be explicit or implicit.

The Borrowed Copula: 'Huwa' as a Linking Tool
Explains the borrowing of 'huwa' as a copula and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'huwa' is used as a copula and defend its status as a logical tool against the objection that it is a noun.

Core Idea
Arabic borrowed 'huwa' because it lacked a non-temporal copula; its original noun status does not prevent it from functioning as a tool.

The use of the pronoun 'huwa' as a copula may seem odd at first. This lesson delves into the reason logicians borrowed it and how they justified this linguistic transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for a copula
Before borrowing, Arabic had no single word for a non-temporal linking verb. Translators needed such a word for logical precision.
2
Understand the borrowing process
Al-Farabi noted that translators borrowed 'huwa' from pronouns to serve as a copula. This is a form of isti'ara (metaphorical extension).
3
Answer the objection
The objection is that 'huwa' is a noun, not a tool. The response: it was originally a noun but borrowed to indicate a relational meaning (ma'na hukmi), thus it functions as a tool.
4
Distinguish temporal and non-temporal copulas
The copula can be temporal (indicating time) or non-temporal. 'Huwa' is non-temporal, suitable for eternal truths.

قوله : محمولا لأنه أمر جعل محمولا لموضوعه قوله والدال على النسبة: أي اللفظة المذكورة في القضية الملفوظة التي تدل على النسبة الحكمية تسمى ،رابطة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول؛ فإن الرابطة حقيقة هي النسبة ،الحكمية وفي قوله والدال على النسبة" إشارة إلى أن الرابطة أداة؛ لدلالتها على النسبة التي هي معنى حرفي غير مستقل واعلم أن الرابطة قد تذكر في القضية، وقد تحذف فالقضية على الأول تسمى ثلاثيةً وعلى الثاني ثنائية قوله وقد استعير لها "هو": اعلم أن الرابطة تنقسم إلى زمانية تدل على اقتران النسبة الحكمية بأحد الأزمنة الثلاثة، وغير زمانية بخلاف ذلك، وذكر الفارابي أن الحكمة الفلسفية لما نقلت من اللغة اليونانية إلى العربية وجد القوم
جعل محمولا وهو قد يكون كلمة، مثل: زيد يقرب، وقد يكون قضية مثل: زيد أبوه قائم، وقد يكون اسما، مثل: كل إنسان حيوان باسم المدلول: الأولى أن يقول بوصف المدلول؛ فإن الرابطة ليست أسماء للنسبة الحكمية، بل إنما هو وصف له إسماعيل النسبة أي على النسبة التي هي مورد الحكم والإذعان؛ لأنه لم يسم اللفظ الدال على نسبة يربط بها الموضوع بالمحمول رابطة ما لم يعتبر معها الوقوع واللاوقوع، ولفظة "هو" رابطة الإيجاب و لم يعتبروا رابطة السلب استغناء بها مع وجود حرف السلب. (عبد) تحذف أي لم تذكر الرابطة في اللفظ اكتفاء عنها بالحركات التي هي علامات دالة عليها بالالتزام لا بالمطابقة كالرفع في الموضوع والمحمول ؛ فإنه دال على كون أحدهما مبتدأ أو محكوما عليه والآخر خبرا ثابتا أو محكوما به، وهذه الدلالة بالالتزام بالمطابقة؛ إذ الإعراب لم يوضع للربط، بل للدلالة على المعاني المعتورة على المعرب، ويلزمها الربط ويفهم منه المعنى الرابطي. (مرآة الشروح للسلم
استعير: اعلم أن الاستعارة لا بدلها من المستعير والمستعار والمستعار منه المستعير والمستعار والمستعار منه والعجز والافتقار، فشرع في بيان كل منها. فالقوم الناقلون هم المستعيرون والمستعار كلمة "هو" و"هي"، والمستعار منه هو الاسم، وعدم وجدانهم رابطة غير زمانية في كلام العرب عند احتياجهم إليها عجز وافتقار . (عبد) وقد استعير لها هو: جواب عما يقال: إن كون الدال على النسبة الرابطة أداة ممنوع بسند أن "هو" في "زيد" هو قائم" يدل على النسبة، وليس بأداة؛ لأنه اسم فأجاب بقوله: "وقد استعير" "لها" للدلالة على النسبة لفظة "هو" مثلا، يعني أن في أصل الوضع اسم.
"هو" ليس بأداة، ثم بالنقل والاستعارة إنه دل على المعنى النسبي التي هي معنى حرفي، فلا ضير. (عبد)
Assents 88. Names of the parts of the proposition: predicate, and the indicator of the relation is the copula, and 'huwa' has been borrowed for it. His statement: 'predicate' because it is something made a predicate for its subject. His statement: 'and the indicator of the relation': that is, the word mentioned in the verbal proposition that indicates the judgmental relation is called a copula, naming the indicator by the name of the indicated; for the copula in reality is the judgmental relation. And in his statement 'and the indicator of the relation' there is an indication that the copula is a tool, because it indicates the relation which is a letter-meaning, not independent. Know that the copula may be mentioned in the proposition, and it may be omitted. So the proposition in the first case is called three-part, and in the second two-part. His statement: 'and 'huwa' has been borrowed for it': know that the copula is divided into temporal, indicating the conjunction of the judgmental relation with one of the three times, and non-temporal, contrary to that. Al-Farabi mentioned that when philosophical wisdom was translated from the Greek language into Arabic, the people found... made predicate, and it may be a word like 'Zayd is approaching', or it may be a proposition like 'Zayd his father is standing', or it may be a noun like 'every human is an animal'. By the name of the indicated: it is better to say 'by the description of the indicated' because the copula is not a name for the judgmental relation, but rather it is a description of it. Ismail: 'the relation' i.e. the relation that is the object of judgment and assent; because the word indicating a relation by which the subject is linked to the predicate is not called a copula unless occurrence and non-occurrence are considered with it. The word 'huwa' is a copula for affirmation, and they did not consider a copula for negation, being independent by the presence of the negation particle. (Abd) 'omitted' i.e. the copula is not mentioned in the wording, being satisfied by the vowel signs that are indicative of it by implication not by correspondence, like the raising (raf') in the subject and predicate; for it indicates that one of them is a mubtada' or subject of judgment, and the other is a predicate established or judged by it. This indication is by implication, not correspondence; because inflection was not placed for linking, but to indicate the meanings that affect the inflected word, and the linking necessarily accompanies it and the relational meaning is understood from it. (Mir'at al-Shuruh lil-Sullam) 'borrowed': know that borrowing necessarily requires a lender, a borrowed thing, and a thing borrowed from; the lenders, the borrowed thing, and the thing borrowed from, and inability and need. So he proceeded to explain each of them. The people who translated are the lenders, the borrowed thing is the word 'huwa' and 'hiya', and the thing borrowed from is the noun. Their not finding a non-temporal copula in the speech of the Arabs when they needed it is inability and need. (Abd) 'And 'huwa' has been borrowed for it': a response to what is said: that the indicator of the relation being a tool is denied based on the fact that 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qa'im' indicates the relation, and it is not a tool because it is a noun. So he answered by saying: 'and 'huwa' has been borrowed for it', i.e., for indicating the relation, the word 'huwa', for example, meaning that in the original position it is a noun. 'Huwa' is not a tool originally, then by transmission and borrowing it came to indicate the relational meaning which is a letter-meaning, so there is no harm. (Abd)
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the three parts of a proposition: subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), and copula (rabita). The copula is the term that indicates the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-hukmiyya) between subject and predicate. The author explains that the copula may be explicit (making a three-part proposition) or implicit (two-part proposition). For explicit copula, the word 'huwa' is often used, but it is borrowed from its original use as a pronoun. Al-Farabi is cited noting that when Greek philosophy was translated into Arabic, translators borrowed 'huwa' to serve as a copula because Arabic lacked a non-temporal linking word. The copula can be temporal (indicating time) or non-temporal. The passage also clarifies that the copula is a tool (adāt) because it indicates a relational meaning, which is a letter-meaning (ma'na ḥarfī), not an independent concept. When the copula is omitted, the proposition is two-part, and the relational meaning is understood from the inflectional endings (e.g., raf' on subject and predicate). The objection that 'huwa' is a noun not a tool is answered: it is a noun originally but borrowed for the relational meaning, so it functions as a copula without harm.

الرابطة الزمانية
الرابطة غير الزمانية

Key Takeaway

Definition of Proposition and the Circularity Objection
Defines proposition and rebuts circularity charge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a proposition (subject and predicate) and explain why defining it by truth/falsity does not lead to circularity.

Core Idea
Truth is direct correspondence, not dependent on 'report'; definition stands without circularity.

This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) as a statement that admits truth or falsity, composed of a subject and predicate. A key objection is that this definition is circular because truth/falsity are defined in terms of 'report' (khabar), which is synonymous with proposition. The author rebuts this by showing that truth/falsity are directly understood as correspondence to reality. We will walk through the definition and the rebuttal step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Find the statement: 'al-qadiyya qawlun yahtamilu al-sidq wa al-kidhb'. Note that 'qawl' in logic includes both verbal and mental composites.
2
Extract the Components
The subject (mawdu') is what is judged upon, and the predicate (mahmul) is what is affirmed or denied. In a categorical proposition, the judgment is either affirmative or negative.
3
Understand Truth and Falsity
Truth is correspondence (mutabaqa) to reality (al-waqi'), falsity is lack of correspondence. This definition does not require prior knowledge of 'report' or 'proposition'.
4
Analyse the Circularity Objection
The objection: defining proposition by truth/falsity is circular because truth/falsity are defined as correspondence of a 'report' (khabar) to reality, and 'report' is synonymous with proposition.
5
Follow the Rebuttal
The rebuttal states that truth/falsity are known by direct correspondence; the concept of report is not needed. Even if report is the subject, correspondence primarily applies to the relation (nisba) in the proposition, not the report itself.

Source Text

القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب، فإن كان الحكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه فحملية موجبة أو سالبة، ويسمى المحكوم عليه موضوعا والمحكوم به محمولا.
قوله: القضية قول القول في عرف هذا الفن يقال للمركب، سواء كان مركبا معقولا أو ملفوظا، فالتعريف يشمل القضية المعقولة والملفوظة. قوله: يحتمل الصدق والكذب: الصدق هو المطابقة للواقع، والكذب هو اللامطابقة له، وهذا المعنى لا يتوقف معرفته على معرفة الخبر والقضية، فلا يلزم الدور. قوله موضوعاً: لأنه وُضِعَ وعين ليحكم عليه.
القضية إلخ: وأنت تعلم أن المقصود في المنطق معرفة الموصل إلى التصور وهو المعرف، ومعرفة الموصل إلى التصديق وهو الحجة. فلما فرغ عن بيان الأول شرع في بيان الثاني، وهو قسمان :قريب وهو الحجة؛ لأنه الموصل إلى التصديق بلا واسطة وبعيد وهو القضية؛ لكونها جزء الحجة، وقدمها لتوقف القريب عليها؛ لتركبه منها. (عبد) القول: دفع لما يتوهم من شهرة القول في اللفظ من أن تعريف القضية بهذا مختص بالقضية الملفوظة، يعني نعم! أن القول بحسب اللغة يختص باللفظ ولهذا اشتهر فيه لكنه في اصطلاح المنطقيين شامل للملفوظ والمعقول. (عبد) الصدق بالنظر إلى مفهومها مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية الموضوع والمحمول وغير ذلك، فلا يرد أن القضايا البديهية الأولية كاجتماع النقيضين محال لا تحتمل الكذب والقضايا التي يحكم العقل بكذبها كـ"السماء تحتنا" لا تحتمل الصدق . ( إسماعيل)
وهذا المعنى إلخ: دفع للاعتراض المشهور على تعريف القضية بلزوم الدور بأن الصدق والكذب مطابقة الخبر للواقع وعدم مطابقة له والخبر والقضية مترادفان، فتوقفت القضية على الصدق والكذب المتوقفين على الخبر، وهذا هو الدور، وحاصل الدفع أن معرفتهما بنفس المطابقة للواقع واللامطابقة له، فلا دور. فإن قيل: فاعل المطابقة واللامطابقة إنما هو الخبر، فالدور باق، قلنا: لا نسلم الحصر لجريانهما في غير الخبر أيضاً؛ فإن التصور قد يطابق الصورة وقد لا يطابقه كما بيّن، وإن سلمناه فنقول: فاعلها في الحقيقة النسبة؛ لأن المطابقة أولا وبالذات للنسبة وثانيا وبالعرض للخبر؛ لاشتماله عليها، ويعلم من تعريف القضية أنها لابد وأن يكون حكاية عن المحكي عنه. (عبد)
Chapter on Assents. The proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsity. If the judgment in it concerns the affirmation or negation of something for something, then it is a categorical affirmative or negative. The subject of judgment is called 'subject' and the predicate is called 'predicate'. His statement: 'The proposition is a statement': 'statement' in the custom of this art is said of a composite, whether it is a mental composite or a verbal composite, so the definition includes both mental and verbal propositions. His statement: 'admits truth and falsity': truth is correspondence to reality, and falsity is lack of correspondence to it. This meaning does not require knowledge of report and proposition to be known, so there is no circularity. His statement: 'subject': because it is placed and specified to be judged upon. 'The proposition etc.': And you know that the aim in logic is knowledge of what leads to conception, which is the definer, and knowledge of what leads to assent, which is the proof. When he finished explaining the first, he began explaining the second. It is of two types: immediate, which is the proof because it leads to assent directly, and remote, which is the proposition because it is part of the proof. He presented it because the immediate depends on it, since it is composed of it. (Abd) 'Statement': a rebuttal of what might be imagined from the fame of 'statement' in language that the definition of proposition by this is specific to the verbal proposition. That is, yes! 'Statement' in language is specific to the word, and for this reason it is famous in it, but in the terminology of logicians it includes both the verbal and the mental. (Abd) 'Truth' with regard to its concept, ignoring the particularity of subject and predicate and otherwise, so it cannot be objected that self-evident primary propositions like the impossibility of two contradictories cannot admit falsity, and propositions that the mind judges to be false like 'the sky is beneath us' cannot admit truth. (Ismail) 'And this meaning etc.': a rebuttal of the famous objection to the definition of proposition by the charge of circularity, that truth and falsity are correspondence of the report to reality and lack of correspondence, and report and proposition are synonymous. Thus the proposition depends on truth and falsity, which depend on the report, and this is circularity. The gist of the rebuttal is that knowledge of them is by correspondence to reality and lack of correspondence, so no circularity. If it is said: the agent of correspondence and lack of correspondence is only the report, so circularity remains, we say: we do not accept the exclusivity, because they also occur in other than the report; for conception may correspond to the form or not, as explained. And if we accept it, we say: the agent in reality is the relation (nisba), because correspondence is firstly and essentially for the relation, and secondly and accidentally for the report because it includes it. And it is known from the definition of proposition that it must be a narration about the narrated-about. (Abd)
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) and addresses a common objection. The author begins by stating that a proposition is a statement (qawl) that admits truth and falsity. He clarifies that 'statement' in logic includes both mental and verbal composites, so the definition covers propositions in the mind and in speech. Then he explains that truth is correspondence to reality, and falsity is lack of correspondence. This definition does not lead to circularity because truth and falsity can be known without first knowing what a 'report' (khabar) or 'proposition' is. The commentator notes that the aim of logic is to understand what leads to conception (tasawwur) and what leads to assent (tasdiq). The proposition is the remote means to assent, while proof is the immediate means. The passage then defends against the objection that defining proposition in terms of truth/falsity is circular because truth/falsity are defined in terms of report, and report is synonymous with proposition. The rebuttal states that truth/falsity are defined directly by correspondence, not by report. Even if one insists that report is the object of truth, correspondence primarily applies to the relation (nisba) in the proposition, not the report itself. Thus the definition stands.

Key Takeaway
