Definition of the General Absolute and Its Composition with Possibility
Commentator defines al-mutlaqa al-amma and explains its composition with al-mumkina al-amma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-amma) and explain its composition with the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-amma).

Core Idea
The general absolute is actual at some time without necessity; its composition with possibility negates necessity.

In this passage, the commentator defines the general absolute proposition and then shows how it combines with the general possible proposition to form a compound that denies necessity. Understanding this definition is crucial for classifying propositions in Islamic logic, especially when analyzing modal arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
The general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-amma) is defined as 'actuality of the relation and its existence at some time' without necessity. Note that it includes non-necessity.
Example given: 'Every human is actually breathing' – this is true at some time but not necessary.
2
Identify the components of the composition
The existential non-necessary proposition is composed of a general absolute (affirmative) and a general possible (negative). The general possible negates necessity.
The compound is: 'Every human is actually breathing, not (necessarily)' i.e., 'No human is breathing by general possibility'.
3
Distinguish essential vs. descriptive non-permanence
The restriction uses essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati), not descriptive. Descriptive non-permanence would contradict descriptive permanence.
Essential non-permanence means the predicate does not hold always in the essence; descriptive refers to a specific description.
4
Recognize why other compositions are not considered
Although temporal absolutes could be restricted by descriptive non-permanence, such compositions are not accepted in this logical tradition.
The commentator states that this composition is 'not considered by them'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام المركبات لأن معنى المطلقة العامة هو فعلية النسبة ووجودها في وقت من الأوقات ولاشتمالها على اللاضرورة فالوجودية اللاضرورية هي المطلقة العامة المقيدة باللاضرورة الذاتية، نحو: كل إنسان متنفس بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإمكان العام، فهي مركبة من مطلقة عامة وممكنة عامة، إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة أو باللادوام الذاتي، إنما قيد اللادوام بالذاتي؛ لأن تقييد العامتين باللادوام الوصفي غير صحيح؛ ضرورة تنافي اللادوام بحسب الوصف مع الدوام بحسب الوصف. نعم يمكن تقييد الوقتيتين المطلقتين باللادوام الوصفي أيضاً لكن هذا التركيب غير معتبر عندهم
Divisions of Compound Propositions. Because the meaning of the general absolute is the actuality of the relation and its existence at some time, and its inclusion of non-necessity. So the existential non-necessary is the general absolute restricted by essential non-necessity, e.g., 'Every human is actually breathing, not necessarily' i.e., 'No human is breathing by general possibility'. So it is composed of a general absolute and a general possible, one affirmative and the other negative. Or it is by essential non-permanence. He only restricted non-permanence by 'essential' because restricting the two general propositions by descriptive non-permanence is incorrect, due to the necessary contradiction between non-permanence with respect to description and permanence with respect to description. Yes, it is possible to restrict the two temporal absolutes by descriptive non-permanence as well, but this composition is not considered by them.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the general absolute proposition (المطلقة العامة) and explains its composition with the general possible proposition. The commentator begins by stating the meaning: the general absolute is actuality of the predication at some time, without necessity. For example, 'Every human is actually breathing' is not necessary because it is not that every human breathes by general possibility (i.e., it could be otherwise). Thus, the existential non-necessary is the general absolute restricted by essential non-necessity. The composition yields a compound proposition: a general absolute (affirmative) and a general possible (negative) – essentially the denial of necessity. Then the commentator clarifies why restriction is with essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي) rather than descriptive non-permanence, because descriptive non-permanence contradicts descriptive permanence which is present in the two general propositions. Finally, he notes that temporal absolutes can be restricted by descriptive non-permanence, but such constructions are not considered in this science.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة العامة
اللاضرورة الذاتية
اللادوام الذاتي

C) A proposition that denies possibility entirely.
D) A proposition that is restricted by descriptive non-permanence.

Key Takeaway
The general absolute proposition is actual at some time and includes non-necessity; its composition with the general possible yields a compound that denies necessity.

Tamthīl: Definition and Its Two Senses
Definition of tamthīl and resolution of tasāmuh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of tamthīl (the process and the argument) and explain why the definition only covers the process.

Core Idea
A single Arabic term can refer both to a mental process and to the argument that uses it.

This passage introduces the concept of tamthīl, the analogical argument used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology. The author defines it as a statement of participation in a cause, but then notes a leniency because the term can refer to two different things. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid confusion when studying analogical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definition
Find the term being defined (al-tamthīl) and the definition given: 'bayān mushārakat juz'ī li juz'ī fī 'illat al-hukm' (a statement of the participation of one particular in another in the cause of the ruling).
The definition describes an act of stating, not the argument itself.
2
Extract the components of the definition
Notice that the definition involves two particulars sharing a cause ('illa). This is the core of the analogical process.
3
Detect the apparent leniency (tasāmuh)
The author points out that the term tamthīl seems to refer to the argument, but the definition describes a process. This mismatch is the tasāmuh.
The leniency is only apparent; it will be resolved.
4
Understand the resolution: two meanings
The commentator explains that tamthīl has two technical senses: the masdar meaning (the process of likening/statement) and the hujja (the argument). The definition is for the first meaning only.
This parallels the term 'aks which also has a process and a product meaning.
5
See how the second meaning is derived
The second meaning (the argument) is known by analogy (muqāyasa) with 'aks: just as 'aks can mean both substitution and the resulting proposition, so tamthīl can mean both the process and the argument.
This resolves the tasāmuh: the definition is valid for the process meaning.

Commentary
This passage defines tamthīl (analogy) and resolves an apparent leniency (tasāmuh) in its definition. The commentator begins by quoting the matn definition: 'tamthīl is a statement of the participation of one particular in another in the cause of the ruling, so that it is established in it.' He then explains that the expression 'so that it is established in it' means the ruling is established in the first particular through likening (tashbīh). The example of nabidh and wine is given: nabidh is forbidden because wine is forbidden, and the cause (ʿilla) of intoxication is shared. However, the commentator notes that there is a leniency in the definition because the term 'tamthīl' properly refers to the argument itself, not just the process of likening. He then clarifies on page 195 that tamthīl has two technical meanings: the masdar meaning (the act of likening and stating) and the argument (hujja) that contains that likening. The definition given applies to the first meaning, while the second meaning is understood by analogy (muqāyasa) to the case of ʿaks (conversion). This resolves the tasāmuh because the definition is for the conceptual meaning, not the argument.

Why the Descriptive Reading of Istiqrā' Avoids Definition by the Broader
Author explains why the descriptive reading of the matn is preferable for defining istiqrā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the descriptive (tawsīf) from the additive (idāfa) reading of a definition and explain why the tawsīf reading is preferred in defining istiqrā'.

Core Idea
A definition that includes more than its intended scope fails—tawsīf saves istiqrā' from that error.

In defining key logical terms, even a slight grammatical shift can change the scope of a definition. The commentator here argues that the phrase 'universal ruling' in the matn should be read as a descriptive construction (tarkīb tawsīfī) rather than an additive one (idāfa). Why? Because the additive reading would make the definition include cases where induction yields a particular ruling—which is not part of conventional induction—thus falling into the error of definition by the broader (al-ta'rīf bil-a'amm). This lesson walks through the commentator's reasoning and its implications for precise definition in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: definition by the broader
Understand that a definition by the broader (al-ta'rīf bil-a'amm) defines a term using a more general concept that includes things outside the intended meaning. The author (al-muṣannif) rejects such definitions.
This is a key principle in definition theory: a valid definition must exactly cover all and only instances of the defined term.
2
Compare two readings of 'universal ruling'
The matn says 'universal ruling' (ḥukm kullī). If read as an additive construction (idāfa: 'ruling of universality'), it means any ruling that is universal, but the definition of istiqrā' would then include any scanning that results in a universal ruling, even if the scanning itself is not for a universal—this is too broad. If read as descriptive (tawsīf: 'a universal ruling'), it specifies that the ruling sought is universal, restricting the definition to induction that seeks a universal.
In Arabic grammar, the difference is subtle but logically significant.
3
See the consequence of the additive reading
The additive reading would make the definition of istiqrā' include scanning that yields a particular ruling, because 'ruling of universality' is a universal property that could apply to a particular ruling (since a particular ruling is still a ruling). This violates the requirement that istiqrā' only yields universal rulings.
The commentator notes that even if one takes the additive reading but restricts it by the known fact that induction yields only universals, it still creates an illusion of definition by the broader.
4
Recognize the advantage of the descriptive reading
The descriptive reading avoids the illusion entirely because it explicitly says 'a universal ruling'—the adjective 'universal' directly modifies 'ruling', so only rulings that are universal are considered. This matches the intended scope.
Hence the descriptive reading is 'better in terms of understanding and correct perception'.
5
Note the two meanings of istiqrā' by analogy
Just as 'aks means both the act of substitution and the resulting proposition, istiqrā' means both the process of scanning (tafāḥ) and the argument (hujja) that contains that scanning. The matn's definition applies to the first meaning; the second is understood by analogy.
This dual meaning is common in logical terms and must be kept in mind when reading definitions.

Dual Meanings of Tamthil and Istiqra'
Expansion on the dual meanings of tamthil and istiqra' with defense of the author's definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two technical meanings of tamthil and istiqra' (concept vs. argument) and explain why the author's definition by cause is valid.

Core Idea
Tamthil and istiqra' each name both a mental process and the argument it produces.

In logic, many terms have a dual nature: they denote both an intellectual operation and the product of that operation. Just as 'aks (conversion) refers to both the act of substituting and the resulting proposition, tamthil (analogical reasoning) and istiqra' (induction) each have two meanings. This lesson unpacks that distinction and examines why the author chose to define them by their process rather than their product.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parallel with 'aks
Recognize that 'aks has two meanings: the verbal noun 'substitution' (a concept) and the proposition resulting from that substitution (an argument). This sets the pattern for tamthil and istiqra'.
2
Extract the two meanings of tamthil
From the passage, find that tamthil is applied to (1) the 'mentioned comparison and exposition' (a concept) and (2) the argument in which that comparison occurs. List each meaning.
3
Extract the two meanings of istiqra'
Similarly, istiqra' is applied to (1) 'examination' (a concept) and (2) the argument in which that examination occurs. Confirm both are present.
4
Understand the author's choice
The author defines tamthil and istiqra' by their first meaning (the process). The commentator defends this as a definition by cause (the process) rather than by effect (the argument). Notice that this is acceptable because the first meaning is a concept, not an argument division.
5
Contrast with the famous definition
The passage hints that the famous definition defines by the effect (the argument). Compare: the author's definition is by cause, not loose, while the other would be loose if applied here. Understand that both are valid in their contexts.

Each of them in the first meaning is not among the divisions of argument but among concepts, so there is no harm in defining it by concept. Indeed! If his intention by each of them was what is a division of argument, his definition by what was mentioned would be considered loose. So what he mentioned is a definition of tamthil, etc. There remains something: that the author did not mention the second meaning. The answer is that it is known by analogy. Yes! Leaving the definition by analogy is not recommended, especially if the mentioned is intended secondarily and the left is intended primarily; because it is at the beginning of expounding the divisions of argument, so he should have defined what is among its divisions. This, i.e., know this when you have known, and he did not say 'take this' because there is an offensiveness in it not hidden from one with the least insight. However, it is not hidden that if it is said: the author's definition of induction and analogy by examination and exposition is a definition by cause, contrary to the famous definition of them as will come in the following gloss; for it is a definition by their resulting effect. The first occurs in many cases, like their definition of contradiction as difference and 'aks as substitution, contrary to the second. So the two loosenesses differ, and it was a reason for their deviating from the famous, and he did not settle on what he fled from. Understand.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition of tamthil (analogical reasoning) and istiqra' (induction) in the context of the five arts of logic. The commentator begins by drawing a parallel to 'aks (conversion), which has two technical meanings: the act of substituting (verbal noun) and the resulting proposition. Similarly, tamthil and istiqra' each have two meanings: a cognitive process (a concept) and the argument resulting from that process. The author's definitions focus on the first meaning (the process), and the commentator defends this by arguing that since these are not divisions of argument but concepts, there is no looseness. The commentator acknowledges that the author did not define the second meaning (the argument) and explains that it can be derived by analogy. He notes that defining the second meaning is more appropriate in a section about argument divisions, but the author's approach is still valid because he defines by the cause (the process) rather than the effect (the argument). This is contrasted with the famous definitions which define by the effect. The passage ends with a note on the author's phrasing.

The Tautology Problem in Defining Tamthil and Istiqra'
Commentator critiques tautology in famous definitions and the author's alternatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify tautological definitions in Islamic logic and evaluate whether an alternative definition avoids the same flaw.

Core Idea
A definition that repeats its own result is tautological; fleeing one tautology may lead to another.

In this lesson, we examine the commentator's critique of the famous definitions of istiqra' and tamthil, and the author's attempt to provide alternatives. The commentator argues that the new definitions still suffer from the same kind of tautology (tasamuh). Understanding this critique sharpens our ability to evaluate definitions in Islamic logic and avoid circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the famous definitions
Locate the definitions of istiqra' and tamthil that the commentator refers to as 'famous'. They are: istiqra' is judgment on a universal due to its existence in most particulars; tamthil is judgment on a particular sharing with another particular in the cause of the ruling.
2
Understand the critique of tautology
The commentator says these definitions contain 'tasamuh' (tolerance/tautology) because the judgment mentioned in the definition is exactly the result or conclusion of that reasoning process. Essentially, they define the term by restating what it produces.
3
Examine the author's alternative definitions
The author deviated from the famous definitions to avoid this tautology, proposing new definitions that he believed were free from suspicion. The text alludes to these as 'al-madhkur' (the mentioned) but does not quote them here – likely defined earlier in the matn.
The exact alternative definitions are not in this chunk; you may need to refer to the matn.
4
Evaluate the commentator's counter-critique
The commentator argues that the author's new definitions also contain tautology, just of a different form. He uses the proverb 'Is it not a return to what he fled from?' to show that the author escaped one tautology only to fall into another.
The commentator's point: both definitions share the same fundamental flaw of being tautological.
5
Reflect on the lesson for defining terms
A valid definition in Islamic logic must not simply restate the result or the process. The definiens should capture the essence (genus and differentia) without circularity. This episode shows how careful scholars were to avoid tautology in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف التمثيل وحكمه
والعمدة في طريقه الدوران والترديد.
عن المشهور إلى المذكور دفعا لتوهم هذا التسامح وهل هو إلا كرّ على ما فرّ عنه. قوله: والعمدة في طريقه إلخ: اعلم أنه لابد في التمثيل من مقدمات: الأول: أن الحكم ثابت في الأصل أعني المشبه به الثانية: أن علة الحكم في الأصل الوصف الكذائي. الثالثة: أن ذلك الوصف موجود في الفرع أعني المشبه؛ فإنه إذا تحقق العلم بهذه المقدمات الثلاث ينتقل الذهن إلى كون الحكم ثابتاً في الفرع أيضاً وهو المطلوب من التمثيل، ثم إن المقدمة الأولى والثالثة ظاهرتان في كل تمثيل، وإنما الإشكال في الثانية، وبيانها بطرق متعددة فصلوها في كتب أصول الفقه، والمصنف ذكر ما هو العمدة من بينها،
عن المشهور أعني الاستقراء هو الحكم على كلي لوجوده في أكثر جزئياته، والتمثيل هو الحكم على جزئي مشارك لجزئي آخر في علة الحكم عليه. ولما كان في كل من التعريفين المشهورين تسامح؛ إذ الحكمان المذكوران في تعريف الاستقراء والتمثيل عين نتيجتهما لأنفسهما عدل المصنف عنهما وتركهما واستنبط منهما تعريفين لا يتوهم عنهما هذا التسامح فعلة العدول دفع التوهم و به لم يعلم أن في ما ذكره من التعريفين أيضاً تسامحا، والمصنف فر عن تسامح وكر إلى تسامح آخر. فالتسامحان وإن تغايرا لكنهما مشتركان في أصل التسامح كما لا يخفى. وليت شعري المراد من كلمة هذا في قوله لهذا التوهم كما وقع في أكثر النسخ التوهم المذكور. هذا غاية ما سنح لي في تحرير كلام الشارح وبيان مرامه ولعل عند غيري أحسن من هذا وهل هو إلا كر: يعني ليس هذا العدول إلا رجوعا على ما فرعنه، وهو التسامح كما عرفت. وهذا مثل مشهور يضرب لمن ترك أمرا احترازا عن بلاء فيه واختار ففاجأ في ما اختار بلاء سواء كان عين البلاء الأول أو بلاء آخر.
واعلم أن "كر" على وزن "فر" ماض معروف بمعنى رجع، ثم هو إما باق على الفعلية؛ لأن الفعل قد يحذف بعد إلا مثل: ما أنت إلا سيراً أي تسير سيرا، و"ما" مصدر بتقدير أن المصدرية أو بدونه كما في تسمع بالمعيدي خير من أن تراه. أقول: لعل رواية هذا التطويل بلا طائل بلغ المحشي المدقق، وإلا فعند الدراية أنه مصدر فلا يلزم التطويل الخالي عن التحصيل، ولو قال قائل هذا المثل: بل هذا إلا كرّ على ما كرَّ عنه لكان له عطف؛ لأن الكر إذا كان صلته "على" كان بمعنى العطف، وإذا كان صلته "عن" كان بمعنى الرجوع، فكان المعنى على هذا عطف على ما رجع عنه. (عبد)
Definition of Tamthil and Its Ruling: And the primary [method] in its path is co-variation and sifting. From the well-known to the mentioned, to repel the misconception of this tolerance, and is it not a return to what he fled from? His statement: 'And the primary in its path etc.': Know that in tamthil, premises are necessary: first, that the ruling is established in the original, i.e., the analogized-from; second, that the cause of the ruling in the original is such-and-such attribute; third, that that attribute exists in the branch, i.e., the analogized-to; for when knowledge of these three premises is realized, the mind transfers to the establishment of the ruling in the branch as well, which is the goal of tamthil. Then the first and third premises are apparent in every tamthil; the difficulty is only in the second, and its demonstration is through multiple methods elaborated in books of usul al-fiqh. The author mentioned what is primary among them. From the well-known, i.e., istiqra' is judgment on a universal due to its existence in most of its particulars, and tamthil is judgment on a particular sharing with another particular in the cause of the ruling upon it. Since in each of the two famous definitions there is tolerance, because the two judgments mentioned in the definition of istiqra' and tamthil are exactly their results themselves, the author deviated from them and left them, and deduced from them two definitions that are not suspected of this tolerance. So the reason for the deviation is repelling the misconception, and it is not known that in what he mentioned of the two definitions there is also tolerance. The author fled from one tolerance and returned to another tolerance. The two tolerances, though different, share in the essence of tolerance, as is not hidden. Would that I knew the meaning of 'this' in his statement 'for this misconception' — as occurs in most copies, the mentioned misconception. This is the utmost that occurred to me in editing the commentator's words and explaining his intent; perhaps with others there is better than this. 'And is it not a return' means this deviation is only a return to what he fled from, i.e., tolerance, as you know. This is a famous proverb applied to one who leaves a matter to avoid a trial in it, and chooses [another], then he is struck in what he chose with a trial — whether the same trial or another. And know that 'karra' has the weight of 'farra', a past tense verb meaning 'he returned'. Then it is either remaining as a verb, because the verb may be omitted after 'illa' like 'ma anta illa sayran' i.e., 'you are only traveling', and 'ma' is a verbal noun with the estimation of 'an' or without it, as in 'tasma' bi'l-mu'ayyidi khayrun min an tarahu'. I say: perhaps this verbose elaboration without substance reached the careful glossator; otherwise, according to knowledge, it is a verbal noun so there is no need for the lengthy discussion without result. If someone said this proverb: 'Is this not a return to what he returned from?' it would have a different aspect, because 'karra' when its particle is 'ala' means 'to attack', and when its particle is 'an' means 'to return'. So the meaning would be 'an attack on what he returned from'. (Abd)

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on tamthil (analogy) and its ruling. The commentator begins by stating that the primary method in the path of tamthil is dawaran (co-variation) and tardid (sifting). He then explains the author's reason for altering the famous definitions of istiqra' and tamthil. The famous definitions are judged to be tautological because they define the terms by restating their results. The author proposed alternative definitions to avoid this misconception, but the commentator argues that his new definitions also contain similar tolerance. The commentator uses the proverb 'is it not a return to what he fled from?' to criticize the author's move, indicating that the new definitions fall into the same pitfall. The passage ends with a grammatical digression on the word 'karra' and its usage, showing the commentator's attention to linguistic precision.

Critiquing al-Dawani's View on the Nature of Problems
Critique of al-Dawani's definition of problems

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the critique of al-Dawani's definition of problems and explain why subjects of problems cannot be external to the subject matter of a science.

Core Idea
A problem is not just a predicate but a proposition whose subject is always rooted in the science's subject matter.

Al-Dawani defined problems (masa'il) as predicates proven by evidence, detaching them from their subjects. The author of this passage challenges that view, insisting that problems are propositions involving both subject and predicate. He argues that every subject of a problem is either the subject matter itself, a species of it, or an essential accident—none lie outside. This critique sharpens our understanding of how sciences are structured: each problem belongs wholly within its domain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the competing definitions
Find the two definitions of 'problem' (mas'ala) in the passage: one as a proposition with subject and predicate, another as a predicate attributed to a subject. Note that al-Dawani opts for the latter.
The author's preferred definition: problems are qadāyā (propositions).
2
Locate the objection to al-Dawani
The author gives two objections: (1) al-Dawani's view does not match the author's explicit wording; (2) if problems were only predicates, then subjects beyond the subject matter would need to be counted as a separate part of the science.
The second objection is the focus of the subsequent discussion.
3
Analyze the reconciliation attempt
The commentary suggests that al-Dawani's words can be interpreted in a way that aligns with the author: propositions are essentially predicates proven for subjects. This shows how logical definitions can be reinterpreted without contradiction.
The passage hints at two possible reconciliations by referring statements to each other.
4
Evaluate the argument about subjects of problems
The author argues that no subject of a problem lies outside the subject matter. Reasons: any subject is either the subject matter itself, a species of it (including the subject matter plus differentia), or an essential accident of it. Example: 'man' is 'animal with rational'—the subject matter (animal) is present. Therefore, the subject is always internal.
This argument nullifies al-Dawani's implication that subjects would be external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يقال: إن المسائل ليست هي مجموع الموضوعات والمحمولات والنسب، بل المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوعات قال المحقق الدواني في حاشية شرح المطالع المسائل هي المحمولات المثبتة بالدليل، وفيه نظر؛ فإنه لا يلايم ظاهر قول المصنف والمسائل هي قضايا كذا وموضوعاتها كذا ومحمولاتها كذا وأيضاً فلو كانت المسائل نفس المحمولات المنسوبة لوجب عد سائر موضوعات المسائل التي هي وراء موضوع العلم جزءًا على حدة، فتدبر. أو يقال إلخ حاصله أن الموضوع ليس داخلا في المسائل؛ فإنها ليست مركبة من الموضوعات والمحمولات من حيث أنها منسوبة إلى الموضوعات كما يظهر من كلام المحقق الدواني في حاشية شرح المطالع، وإذا لم تكن مندرجة تحت المسائل فلا مضايقة في كونه جزءًا على حدة سوى المسائل. إسماعيل) ليست هي مجموع إلخ: أي ليست هي قضايا. قال المحقق الدواني: تأييدا لكون المسائل محمولة منسوبة إلى الموضوعات وفيه أي في الجواب بكون المسائل نفس المحمولات نظر فإنه لا يلايم: فيه إيماء إلى أنه يلايم باطنه إما بإرجاع قول المصنف إلى ما قال المحقق بأن القضايا ليست إلا المحمولات المثبتة الموضوع بالدليل أو بإرجاع قول المحقق إلى ما قال المصنف بأن المحمولات المثبتة للموضوع ليست هي إلا القضايا كما هو الظاهر على الخبير الماهر وأيضاً : أي في الجواب المذكور نظر آخر أيضاً. فتدبر إشارة إلى منع الملازمة تقريره: أنا لا نسلم أن المسائل لو كانت نفس المحمولات المنسوبة إلى الموضوع لزم إلخ بسند أنه ليس شيء من موضوعات المسائل وراء موضوع العلم حتى يلزم عده من أجزاء العلم وراء موضوع العلم؛ فإن موضوعات المسائل اما موضوع العلم أو نوع منه أو عرض ذاتي له، ولا شيء من الموضوعات خال عن موضوع العلم. أما على الأول فظاهر ، وأما إذا كانت نوعا منه أو عرضا ذاتيا لموضوع العلم؛ فإن موضوع العلم موجود هناك؛ لأن نوع الشيء عبارة عن ذلك الشيء مع فصل منوع له، ألا ترى أن الإنسان هو الحيوان مع الناطق. والمركب إما مركب من موضوع العلم وعرض ذاتي له أو من نوعه وعرض ذاتي له، فلا خفاء في وجوده حينئذ. وأما إذا كانت عرضا ذاتيا لموضوع العلم فلأن العرض الذاتي من غير ملاحظة ما هو معروضة لا يقع موضوع المسألة، ألا ترى أن كل متحرك فله ميل طبيعي، فإنه مثاله كما سيأتي، فليس في موضوعات المسائل موضوع وراء موضوع العلم على أي حال، وليس لغيره في المسائل محال.
Or it is said: The problems are not the total of subjects and predicates and relations, but rather the predicates attributed to the subjects. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said in his hashya on Sharh al-Matali': 'Problems are the predicates proven by evidence.' And there is a problem with it; for it does not accord with the outward meaning of the author's statement: 'Problems are such-and-such propositions, and their subjects are such-and-such, and their predicates are such-and-such.' Also, if problems were themselves the attributed predicates, then it would be necessary to count the rest of the subjects of the problems that are beyond the subject matter of the science as a separate part. So reflect. 'Or it is said etc.' Its gist is that the subject is not included in the problems; for they are not composed of subjects and predicates in that they are attributed to the subjects, as appears from al-Dawani's words in Sharh al-Matali'. And if they are not included under the problems, then there is no harm in its being a separate part apart from the problems. (Isma'il) 'They are not the total etc.' i.e., they are not propositions. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said in support that the problems are predicates attributed to the subjects. And in it, i.e., in the answer that the problems are themselves the predicates, there is a problem; for it does not accord: there is an indication that it accords in its inner meaning, either by referring the author's statement to what al-Muhaqqiq said—that the propositions are only the predicates proven for the subject by evidence—or by referring al-Muhaqqiq's statement to what the author said—that the predicates proven for the subject are only the propositions, as is apparent to the expert. 'And also' i.e., in the mentioned answer there is another problem. 'So reflect' is a reference to denying the implication. Its exposition: We do not grant that if problems were themselves the predicates attributed to the subject, it would be necessary etc., on the basis that there is nothing among the subjects of problems that is beyond the subject matter of the science such that it would be necessary to count it among the parts of the science beyond the subject matter. For the subjects of problems are either the subject matter of the science, or a species of it, or an essential accident of it, and none of the subjects are devoid of the subject matter. As for the first, it is obvious. As for when they are a species of it or an essential accident of the subject matter, then the subject matter is present there; because the species of a thing is that thing with a differentiating differentia. Do you not see that man is animal with rational? The composite is either composed of the subject matter and its essential accident, or of its species and its essential accident, so there is no concealment of its existence then. And as for when they are an essential accident of the subject matter, because the essential accident without observing what is its substrate does not occur as the subject of a problem. Do you not see that 'everything that moves has a natural inclination'—it is an example as will come? So there is no subject among the subjects of problems that is beyond the subject matter in any case, and there is no possibility for anything else in the problems.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement. It opens by presenting an alternative view: problems (masa'il) are not composites of subjects and predicates but merely predicates attributed to subjects. The author cites al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani's hashya defining problems as 'predicates proven by evidence.' The author then raises an objection: this definition contradicts the clear wording of the original author, who explicitly says problems are propositions with their subjects and predicates. Additionally, if problems were predicates alone, then the subjects of those problems that lie outside the subject matter of the science would have to be counted as a separate part of the science, which is problematic. The following commentary (starting 'او يقال الخ') explains that the subject is not included in the problems, so there is no harm in it being a separate part. Later, the commentary elaborates on the possible interpretations of al-Dawani's words to reconcile them with the author's. The core argument is that all subjects of problems are either the subject matter itself, its species, or its essential accident, so nothing is truly outside. The example 'man is animal with rational' illustrates how a species includes the subject matter (genus). Thus, the objection that subjects would be separate is invalid. This passage demonstrates a standard logical technique: clarifying definitions by examining the relationship between conceptual parts.

المحمولات المنسوبة
العرض الذاتي

Definition of Assertoric Principles and Three Interpretations
Commentator defines mabadi' tasdiqiyya and discusses three interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of mabadi' tasdiqiyya and identify the three interpretations regarding the inclusion of the subject's definition as a part of a science.

Core Idea
Assertoric principles are the premises forming syllogisms; definitions of the subject are counted separately by tolerance.

In this passage, the commentator on Tahzeeb al-Mantiq clarifies what is meant by the assertoric principles (mabadi' tasdiqiyya) and addresses why the definition and assent regarding the subject are considered separate parts of a science. He defends the author's wording 'upon which the syllogisms are based' against an objection by explaining that it is a definition by the broader term (al-a'mm) focusing on the essential accident of being a part. Three interpretations for counting the subject's definition are then discussed, showing the flexibility in organizing scientific principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of assertoric principles
Find the sentence that defines mabadi' tasdiqiyya. It states they are premises that are self-evident or taken from proofs, upon which the syllogisms of the science and its problems are based.
These premises are propositions (qadaya).
2
Understand the objection to the definition
An implied objection is raised: 'Allama stated that assertoric principles are specifically the premises that compose syllogisms. The author's phrase 'upon which they are based' could include not only the premises but also external proofs, making the definition too broad.
The base text's wording is defended.
3
Analyze the response: definition by the broader term
The response explains that the definition is either a verbal definition (tafsir) using the broader term (al-a'mm), which is permissible, or a definition by an essential accident (al-'arad al-dhati). The intended meaning is the dependence of the whole on its parts, not external conditions.
This clarifies that the premises are parts of the syllogism.
4
Distinguish the three interpretations
The commentator presents three interpretations for why the subject's definition is counted as a separate part: (1) for greater consideration, (2) the assent to the existence of the subject is counted among assertoric principles as a tolerance, (3) the assent to being a subject is counted for insight, being the farthest possibility.
Each interpretation reflects a different emphasis.
5
Synthesize the structure of a science
The parts of a science are its boundaries (hudud), accidents (a'rad), and premises (muqaddimat). The premises are either self-evident or taken from proofs. The definition of the subject is usually a conceptual principle but may be counted separately.
This helps organize any Islamic science.

أجزاء العلوم
وأجزاؤها وأعراضها ومقدمات بينة، أو مأخوذة تبتني عليها قياسات العلم والمسائل، وهي قضايا
وأما على الثاني فيقال: إن تعريف الموضوع وإن كان مندرجا في المبادي التصورية، لكن عد جزءا على حدة لمزيد الاعتبار به كما سبق. وأما على الثالث فيقال بمثل ما مر أو يقال: بأن عد التصديق بوجود الموضوع من المبادي التصديقية كما نقل عن الشيخ تسامح؛ فإن المبادي التصديقية هي القضايا التي يتألف منها قياسات العلم. نص على ذلك العلامة في شرح الكليات، وأيده لكلام الشيخ أيضاً وحينئذٍ فقول المصنف: تبتني عليها قياسات العلم تعريف أو تفسير بالأعم. وأما على الرابع فيقال: إن التصديق بالموضوعية لما توقف عليه الشروع على بصيرة، وكان له مزيد مدخلية في معرفة مباحث العلم وتميزها عما ليس عنه عد جزء من العلم مسامحة، وهذا أبعد المحتملات قوله :أجزائها أي حدود أجزائها إذا كانت الموضوعات مركبة. قوله وأعراضها أي حدود العوارض المثبتة لتلك الموضوعات قوله ومقدمات بينة: المبادي التصديقية إما مقدمات بينة بنفسها أي بديهية أو مقدمات مأخوذة أي نظرية، فالأولى تسمى
فقول المصنف إلخ: جواب عن سؤال مقدر تقرير السؤال: لو كان الأمر كما نص العلامة لم يصح قول المصنف؛ فإن قوله: تبتني إلخ شامل لما يتألف إلخ ولدلائل ما يتألف؛ فإن الدلائل للقياس يصدق عليها أنها ما يبتني عليها القياس، وتقرير الجواب: أن ههنا تعريفا وهو بالأعم جائز عند البعض أو تفسيرا أي تعريفا لفظيا وهو جائز بالأعم عند الأكثر . (برهان ) (الدين تبتني عليها إلخ لأن ابتناء الشيء على الشيء أي توقفه عليه يصدق على أمرين أحدهما توقف الكل على أجزاء وثانيهما: توقف الشيء على أمر خارج عنه أي على شرط؛ فإن الشرط والجزاء مشتركان في التوقف عليهما ومتميزان بالدخول والخروج؛ فإن الجزء يكون داخلا في الكل والشرط خارجا عن المشروط والمبادي التصديقية تكون أجزاء لقياسات العلم لا خارجة عنها، فتعريفها بمقدمات تبتني عليها قياسات العلم تعريف بما هو عرض ذاتي لها ؛ لغرض الامتياز عن بعض ما عداه، فلا امتياز وإن كان في نفسه أعم، لكن المراد ههنا الابتناء الخاص أعني ابتناء الكل على أجزائه.
مأخوذة أي نظرية؛ فإن المراد من المأخوذة هي المأخوذة من الدلائل والمأخوذ من الدليل نظري ألبتة. إسماعيل)
In conclusion: The parts of sciences are their boundaries, their accidents, and premises that are either self-evident or taken from proofs, upon which the syllogisms of the science and its problems are based, and they are propositions. As for the second interpretation: It is said that the definition of the subject, although it is included among the conceptual principles, is counted as a separate part for greater consideration as mentioned before. As for the third interpretation: It is said similar to what has passed, or it is said that counting the assent to the existence of the subject among the assertoric principles is a tolerance mentioned from the Shaykh; for the assertoric principles are the propositions from which the syllogisms of the science are composed. The 'Allama stated that in his commentary on al-Kulliyyat, and supported it from the Shaykh's statement. And then the author's saying: 'upon which the syllogisms of the science are based' is a definition or interpretation by the broader term. As for the fourth interpretation: It is said that the assent to being a subject, because starting with insight depends on it, and it has additional involvement in knowing the topics of the science and distinguishing them from what is not from it, counting it as a part of the science is a tolerance; and this is the farthest of the possibilities. His saying: 'their parts' means the boundaries of their parts when the subjects are composite. His saying: 'and their accidents' means the boundaries of the accidents affirmed for those subjects. His saying: 'and premises that are self-evident': The assertoric principles are either premises that are self-evident in themselves, i.e., intuitive, or premises that are taken, i.e., theoretical. The first are called... Thus the author's saying etc.: a response to an implied question. The question: If the matter is as the 'Allama stated, then the author's statement would not be correct, for his 'upon which they are based' includes both what is composed and the proofs of what is composed; for the proofs of the syllogism are truly described as what the syllogism is based upon. The response: Here there is a definition by the broader term, which is permissible according to some, or an interpretation, i.e., a verbal definition, which is permissible by the broader term according to most. (Burhan) (al-Din) 'upon which they are based' etc. Because the building of a thing upon a thing, i.e., its dependence on it, applies to two things: one is the dependence of the whole on its parts, and the second is the dependence of a thing on something external to it, i.e., a condition; for the condition and the conditioned share in the dependence on them and are distinguished by inclusion and exclusion; for the part is internal to the whole and the condition is external to the conditioned. The assertoric principles are parts of the syllogisms of the science, not external to them, so defining them as premises upon which the syllogisms of the science are based is a definition by an essential accident for the purpose of distinguishing them from some others; there is no distinction even if it is in itself broader, but the intention here is the specific dependence, namely the dependence of the whole on its parts. 'Taken' i.e., theoretical; for what is meant by 'taken' is that which is taken from proofs, and what is taken from proof is always theoretical. (Ismail)
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the principles (mabadi') of a science. It begins by stating that the parts of sciences are boundaries (hudud), accidents (a'rad), and premises (muqaddimat). The premises are either self-evident (bayyina) or taken from proofs (ma'khudha). The commentator then addresses the question of why the definition of the subject is counted as a separate part among the conceptual principles. Three interpretations are given: (1) the definition is counted separately for greater consideration; (2) the assent to the existence of the subject is counted among the assertoric principles as a tolerance attributed to the Shaykh; (3) the assent to being a subject is counted as a separate part for insight. The commentator refines that the assertoric principles are specifically the premises from which syllogisms are composed. He defends the author's wording 'upon which the syllogisms are based' against an objection that it could include both premises and proofs. The response is that it is a definition by the broader term (al-a'mm) which is permissible in verbal definitions, and the intended meaning is the specific dependence of the whole on its parts. Finally, 'taken' (ma'khudha) is explained as theoretical, derived from proofs.

المبادي التصورية
مقدمات بينة

A) They include both the premises and the proofs of the syllogisms.
B) They are the premises that are the parts of the syllogisms.
C) They are the conditions external to the syllogisms.
D) They are the conclusions of the syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The assertoric principles are the premises that serve as parts of the syllogisms of a science, not external conditions; definitions of the subject are included separately by tolerance.

Classification of Premises: Self-Evident, Postulated, and Stolen
Commentator classifies premises by acceptance type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given premise as self-evident, postulated, or stolen based on its epistemic status and the student's acceptance.

Core Idea
A premise's classification depends on the student's attitude: good opinion makes it a postulate; objection makes it stolen.

In the organization of a demonstrative science, premises are divided into self-evident (bayyina) and taken (ma'khudha). The taken premises are further subdivided based on how the student receives them. This lesson explains the key distinction between postulated principles and stolen premises, which hinges on the student's trust or resistance, as illustrated with examples from natural science and geometry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Look at the premise in context. Is it presented as obviously true or is it something the student is asked to accept?
2
Check if it is self-evident
Self-evident premises (bayyina) are known without proof and are called 'well-known sciences' because their truth is apparent. They are not 'taken' but are axioms.
3
Determine the student's attitude if taken
If the premise is not self-evident, it is 'taken'. The classification depends on the student's reaction: if the student accepts it out of good opinion of the teacher, it is a postulated principle (asl mawdu'); if the student objects to it, it is a stolen premise (musadara).
The commentator notes that the same premise can be postulated for one student and stolen for another.
4
Apply to examples
Consider the premise 'Every body has a natural form' in natural science. If a student accepts it on the teacher's authority, it is postulated; if the student challenges it, it is stolen. This distinction helps structure scientific proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تطلب في العلم وموضوعاتها موضوع العلم أو نوع منه أو عرض ذاتي له أو مركب
علوما متعارفة، والثانية إن أذعن بها المتعلم بحسن ظنه بالمعلم سميت أصولا موضوعة، وإن أخذها مع استنكار سمیت مصادرة، ومن ههنا يعلم أن مقدمة واحدة يجوز أن تكون أصلا موضوعا بالنسبة إلى شخص ومصادرة بالقياس إلى آخر قوله موضوع العلم كقولهم في الطبيعي: كل جسم شكل طبيعي قوله : أو عرض ذاتي كقولهم كل متحرك فله ميل قوله أو مركب من الموضوع مع العرض الذاتي كقول المهندس كل مقدار وسط في النسبة فهو ضلع ما يحيط به الطرفان
فله
علوما متعارفة: أما كونها علوما؛ فلأن المراد بمقدمات بينة التصديقات بها وكونها علوماً ظاهر؛ لأن التصديق قسم من العلم، وأما نفس المقدمات فهي قضايا، وقد تحقق في محله أن القضية معلوم، والإذعان ههنا علم وتصديق، والفرق بين التصديق والقضية بالعلم والمعلوم، وأما كونه متعارفة فلشهرة معرفتها؛ لبداهتها. سمیت مصادرة لأنه يصدر بها المسائل التي يتوقف عليها التثبث بالدلائل أولا، ثم يتركب منها قياسات العلم. في الطبيعي أي في العلم الباحث عن الجسم الطبيعي، وقد جعل موضوعا في هذه المسألة أعني كل جسم شكل طبيعي. فله شكل طبيعي أي شكل يقتضيه الطبيعة النوعية والجسم الطبيعي جوهر قابل للانقسام في الجهات الثلاث وعرضه الذاتي الحركة والسكون. شكل طبيعي: أي شكل لحقه من حيث ذاته وطبعه لا باعتبار الأمر الخارج من ذاته كالفاعل وغيره. (إسماعيل) فله ميل: أي ميل طبيعي إلى مركزه، وهذه المسألة أيضاً من العلم الطبيعي، وموضوعها الحركة التي هي ذاتي لموضوع، وهو الجسم الطبيعي كما مر . والميل بفتح الميم وسكون الياء التحتانية بنقطتين: الكيفية التي يكون بها الجسم مدافعاً لما يمانعه عن الحركة إلى جهة ما. كذا عرفه الشيخ الرئيس في رسالة الحدود. من الموضوع: يعني أن قوله أو مركب كلي تحته فردان أحدهما: المركب من موضوع العلم وعرضه الذاتي، وثانيهما: المركب من نوعه وعرضه الذاتي. أما المركب من موضوع العلم ونوعه فهو عين النوع داخل في قوله: أو نوعٌ منه منه؛ لأن نوعه عبارة عن ذلك الموضوع مع الفصل المنوع، وخارج من قوله أو مركب بقرينة المقابلة. فافهم. كل مقدار وسط في النسبة إلخ : اعلم أن موضوع علم الهندسة المقدار، وكونه وسطا في النسبة عرض ذاتي والمقدار عرض يقبل الانقسام، ومعنى كون المقدار وسطا في النسبة عند المهندسين كونه بين مقدارين نسبة ذلك المقدار الوسط إلى أحد ذينك المقدارين، مثل: نسبة المقدار الآخر منهما إلى ذلك المقدار الوسط كالأربعة بين
الاثنين والثمانية؛ فإنها نصف الثمانية كما أن الاثنين نصف لها أو يقال: إن الثمانية ضعف الأربعة كما أن عرض له، =
The premises sought in the science and their subjects are the subject of the science, or a type of it, or an essential accident of it, or a composite. They are well-known sciences. The second, if the student assents to them out of good opinion of the teacher, are called postulated principles (usul mawdu'a); if they are taken while objecting to them, they are called stolen (musadara). From here it is known that a single premise may be a postulated principle for one person and a stolen one for another. His saying: the subject of the science, like their saying in natural science: 'Every body has a natural form.' His saying: or an essential accident, like their saying: 'Everything in motion has a tendency (mayl).' His saying: or a composite of the subject and the essential accident, like the engineer's saying: 'Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion is a side of a figure that encloses the two extremes.' So he says: well-known sciences: As for them being sciences, because what is meant by self-evident premises is the assents (tasdiqat) to them, and them being sciences is apparent, because assent is a kind of knowledge. As for the premises themselves, they are propositions, and it is established in its place that the proposition is something known (ma'lum), and the assent here is knowledge and affirmation, and the difference between assent and proposition is that of knowledge and known. As for them being well-known, it is due to the fame of their knowledge, because of their self-evidence. They are called stolen because issues (masa'il) that require confirmation by proofs are initially produced from them, then the syllogisms of the science are composed from them. In natural science, i.e., in the science investigating the natural body, and the subject in this problem is taken to be 'Every body has a natural form.' Thus it has a natural form, i.e., a form required by the specific nature. The natural body is a substance divisible in three dimensions, and its essential accident is motion and rest. Natural form: a form that attaches to it from its essence and nature, not by consideration of something external to its essence like the agent or other. (Ismail) Thus it has a tendency: i.e., a natural tendency towards its center. This problem is also from natural science, and its subject is motion, which is essential to the subject, i.e., the natural body as previously. And 'mayl' (with fatha on mim and sukun on ya' with two dots) is the quality by which a body repels what opposes it from moving towards a direction. This is how Shaykh al-Ra'is defined it in his Treatise on Definitions. From the subject: meaning that his saying 'or a composite' is a universal under which are two individuals: one is the composite of the subject of the science and its essential accident, and the second is the composite of its type and its essential accident. As for the composite of the subject of the science and its type, it is exactly the type, included under his saying 'or a type of it'; because its type is that subject with the specific differentia, and excluded from his saying 'or a composite' by the context of contrast. Understand. Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion etc.: Know that the subject of geometry is magnitude, and its being a mean in proportion is an essential accident. Magnitude is an accident that accepts division. The meaning of a magnitude being a mean in proportion among engineers is that it is between two magnitudes such that the ratio of that mean magnitude to one of those two magnitudes is like the ratio of the other magnitude to that mean magnitude, like four between two and eight; for it is half of eight just as two is half of it, or it is said that eight is twice four just as...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage classifies the premises (mabadi') of a science according to what their subjects are: they may be the subject of the science itself, a type of that subject, an essential accident of the subject, or a composite of the subject and its accident. It then distinguishes between self-evident premises (which are well-known sciences) and taken premises. The taken premises are further divided: if the student accepts them out of trust in the teacher, they are called 'postulated principles' (usul mawdu'a); if the student takes them while objecting, they are called 'stolen' (musadara). A single premise can be a postulate for one person and a stolen one for another. The commentator then explains examples: in natural science, 'Every body has a natural form' is about the subject itself; 'Everything in motion has a tendency' is about an essential accident; in geometry, 'Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion is a side of a figure that encloses the two extremes' is a composite of subject and accident. The commentary also defines natural body, natural form, and tendency (mayl), citing Shaykh al-Ra'is (Ibn Sina). Finally, it explains the composite types and gives the geometric mean example.

The Subject Matter of Premises in a Science
Commentator lists what premises can be about

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a premise in a demonstrative science is about the subject of the science, a type of it, an essential accident of it, or a composite of these.

Core Idea
Every premise in a science relates to its subject either directly, through a type, an essential accident, or a composite of subject and accident.

Premises in a demonstrative science are not random; they are about the subject matter of that science. The commentator specifies four categories: the subject itself, a type of the subject, an essential accident of the subject, or a composite of the subject and an essential accident. This lesson uses examples from natural science (body, motion) and geometry (magnitude, mean proportion) to illustrate each category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the science and its subject
First, determine the overarching science (e.g., natural science, geometry) and its subject (e.g., natural body, magnitude). The subject is what the science studies in general.
In natural science, the subject is the natural body; in geometry, it is magnitude.
2
Classify the premise based on what it describes
Examine the premise: Does it talk directly about the subject? (e.g., 'Every body has a natural form' is about the subject). Does it talk about a type of the subject? (e.g., 'Every human is rational' if human is a type of body). Does it talk about an essential accident? (e.g., 'Everything in motion has a tendency' - motion is an essential accident of body). Or is it a composite? (e.g., 'Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion is a side...' - magnitude is subject, being a mean is an accident).
The commentator also mentions composite of type and accident, but emphasizes that composite of subject and type reduces to type.
3
Verify with the provided examples
Check the natural science example: 'Every body has a natural form' is directly about the subject. 'Everything in motion has a tendency' is about an essential accident (motion) of the body. The geometry example 'Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion...' is a composite of subject (magnitude) and accident (being a mean).
4
Apply to a new example
Consider a premise in kalam: 'Every accident is temporally originated.' The subject of kalam is the world or existing things, often studied under essence and accidents. 'Accident' is a type of existing thing? Actually, accidents are a type of entity. So this premise would be about a type of subject (existents).

تطلب في العلم وموضوعاتها موضوع العلم أو نوع منه أو عرض ذاتي له أو مركب
علوما متعارفة، والثانية إن أذعن بها المتعلم بحسن ظنه بالمعلم سميت أصولا موضوعة، وإن أخذها مع استنكار سمیت مصادرة، ومن ههنا يعلم أن مقدمة واحدة يجوز أن تكون أصلا موضوعا بالنسبة إلى شخص ومصادرة بالقياس إلى آخر قوله موضوع العلم كقولهم في الطبيعي: كل جسم شكل طبيعي قوله : أو عرض ذاتي كقولهم كل متحرك فله ميل قوله أو مركب من الموضوع مع العرض الذاتي كقول المهندس كل مقدار وسط في النسبة فهو ضلع ما يحيط به الطرفان
فله
علوما متعارفة: أما كونها علوما؛ فلأن المراد بمقدمات بينة التصديقات بها وكونها علوماً ظاهر؛ لأن التصديق قسم من العلم، وأما نفس المقدمات فهي قضايا، وقد تحقق في محله أن القضية معلوم، والإذعان ههنا علم وتصديق، والفرق بين التصديق والقضية بالعلم والمعلوم، وأما كونه متعارفة فلشهرة معرفتها؛ لبداهتها. سمیت مصادرة لأنه يصدر بها المسائل التي يتوقف عليها التثبث بالدلائل أولا، ثم يتركب منها قياسات العلم. في الطبيعي أي في العلم الباحث عن الجسم الطبيعي، وقد جعل موضوعا في هذه المسألة أعني كل جسم شكل طبيعي. فله شكل طبيعي أي شكل يقتضيه الطبيعة النوعية والجسم الطبيعي جوهر قابل للانقسام في الجهات الثلاث وعرضه الذاتي الحركة والسكون. شكل طبيعي: أي شكل لحقه من حيث ذاته وطبعه لا باعتبار الأمر الخارج من ذاته كالفاعل وغيره. (إسماعيل) فله ميل: أي ميل طبيعي إلى مركزه، وهذه المسألة أيضاً من العلم الطبيعي، وموضوعها الحركة التي هي ذاتي لموضوع، وهو الجسم الطبيعي كما مر . والميل بفتح الميم وسكون الياء التحتانية بنقطتين: الكيفية التي يكون بها الجسم مدافعاً لما يمانعه عن الحركة إلى جهة ما. كذا عرفه الشيخ الرئيس في رسالة الحدود. من الموضوع: يعني أن قوله أو مركب كلي تحته فردان أحدهما: المركب من موضوع العلم وعرضه الذاتي، وثانيهما: المركب من نوعه وعرضه الذاتي. أما المركب من موضوع العلم ونوعه فهو عين النوع داخل في قوله: أو نوعٌ منه منه؛ لأن نوعه عبارة عن ذلك الموضوع مع الفصل المنوع، وخارج من قوله أو مركب بقرينة المقابلة. فافهم. كل مقدار وسط في النسبة إلخ : اعلم أن موضوع علم الهندسة المقدار، وكونه وسطا في النسبة عرض ذاتي والمقدار عرض يقبل الانقسام، ومعنى كون المقدار وسطا في النسبة عند المهندسين كونه بين مقدارين نسبة ذلك المقدار الوسط إلى أحد ذينك المقدارين، مثل: نسبة المقدار الآخر منهما إلى ذلك المقدار الوسط كالأربعة بين
الاثنين والثمانية؛ فإنها نصف الثمانية كما أن الاثنين نصف لها أو يقال: إن الثمانية ضعف الأربعة كما أن عرض له، =
The premises sought in the science and their subjects are the subject of the science, or a type of it, or an essential accident of it, or a composite. They are well-known sciences. The second, if the student assents to them out of good opinion of the teacher, are called postulated principles (usul mawdu'a); if they are taken while objecting to them, they are called stolen (musadara). From here it is known that a single premise may be a postulated principle for one person and a stolen one for another. His saying: the subject of the science, like their saying in natural science: 'Every body has a natural form.' His saying: or an essential accident, like their saying: 'Everything in motion has a tendency (mayl).' His saying: or a composite of the subject and the essential accident, like the engineer's saying: 'Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion is a side of a figure that encloses the two extremes.' So he says: well-known sciences: As for them being sciences, because what is meant by self-evident premises is the assents (tasdiqat) to them, and them being sciences is apparent, because assent is a kind of knowledge. As for the premises themselves, they are propositions, and it is established in its place that the proposition is something known (ma'lum), and the assent here is knowledge and affirmation, and the difference between assent and proposition is that of knowledge and known. As for them being well-known, it is due to the fame of their knowledge, because of their self-evidence. They are called stolen because issues (masa'il) that require confirmation by proofs are initially produced from them, then the syllogisms of the science are composed from them. In natural science, i.e., in the science investigating the natural body, and the subject in this problem is taken to be 'Every body has a natural form.' Thus it has a natural form, i.e., a form required by the specific nature. The natural body is a substance divisible in three dimensions, and its essential accident is motion and rest. Natural form: a form that attaches to it from its essence and nature, not by consideration of something external to its essence like the agent or other. (Ismail) Thus it has a tendency: i.e., a natural tendency towards its center. This problem is also from natural science, and its subject is motion, which is essential to the subject, i.e., the natural body as previously. And 'mayl' (with fatha on mim and sukun on ya' with two dots) is the quality by which a body repels what opposes it from moving towards a direction. This is how Shaykh al-Ra'is defined it in his Treatise on Definitions. From the subject: meaning that his saying 'or a composite' is a universal under which are two individuals: one is the composite of the subject of the science and its essential accident, and the second is the composite of its type and its essential accident. As for the composite of the subject of the science and its type, it is exactly the type, included under his saying 'or a type of it'; because its type is that subject with the specific differentia, and excluded from his saying 'or a composite' by the context of contrast. Understand. Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion etc.: Know that the subject of geometry is magnitude, and its being a mean in proportion is an essential accident. Magnitude is an accident that accepts division. The meaning of a magnitude being a mean in proportion among engineers is that it is between two magnitudes such that the ratio of that mean magnitude to one of those two magnitudes is like the ratio of the other magnitude to that mean magnitude, like four between two and eight; for it is half of eight just as two is half of it, or it is said that eight is twice four just as...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage classifies the premises (mabadi') of a science according to what their subjects are: they may be the subject of the science itself, a type of that subject, an essential accident of the subject, or a composite of the subject and its accident. It then distinguishes between self-evident premises (which are well-known sciences) and taken premises. The taken premises are further divided: if the student accepts them out of trust in the teacher, they are called 'postulated principles' (usul mawdu'a); if the student takes them while objecting, they are called 'stolen' (musadara). A single premise can be a postulate for one person and a stolen one for another. The commentator then explains examples: in natural science, 'Every body has a natural form' is about the subject itself; 'Everything in motion has a tendency' is about an essential accident; in geometry, 'Every magnitude that is a mean in proportion is a side of a figure that encloses the two extremes' is a composite of subject and accident. The commentary also defines natural body, natural form, and tendency (mayl), citing Shaykh al-Ra'is (Ibn Sina). Finally, it explains the composite types and gives the geometric mean example.

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of a premise in a demonstrative science must be either the subject of that science, a type of it, an essential accident of it, or a composite of subject and accident.

Interpreting 'Li-Dhatiha' in Essential Accidents
Author defines lazim and arad, clarifies 'li-dhatiha' as including essential accidents and those mediated by co-extensive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of essential accidents (primary and via co-extensive attribute) and explain how the phrase 'li-dhatiha' is interpreted to include both.

Core Idea
'Li-dhatiha' means due to a specific preparedness, including both immediate and mediated essential accidents.

The passage defines predicates of sciences as matters external to subjects, attaching for their essences. But 'li-dhatiha' seems only to cover primary accidents. The commentator reinterprets it as 'due to a specific preparedness', thereby including accidents that attach via a co-extensive attribute. This lesson unpacks that reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial definition
Read the opening: 'أمور خارجة عنها لاحقة لها لذاتها'. Note that 'li-dhatiha' suggests attachment by virtue of essence.
This is the base text claim.
2
Recognize the problem
The commentator observes that this apparently excludes accidents that attach via a co-extensive attribute (musawi), even though they are considered essential by agreement.
The phrase 'بحسب الظاهر' signals this issue.
3
Find the reinterpretation
Some commentators interpret 'li-dhatiha' as 'لاستعداد مخصوص بذواتها' (a specific preparedness in their essences). This broadens the meaning.
This is given as: 'أي لاستعداد مخصوص بذواتها'.
4
Confirm inclusiveness
The commentary states that this interpretation covers both direct attachment and attachment via a co-extensive attribute, because 'اللاحق للشيء لما هو هو' includes all essential accidents.
The generalization is supported by a quote from the author's own commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة أجزاء العلوم ومحمولاتها أمور خارجة عنها لاحقة لها لذواتها. أو من نوعه مع العرض الذاتي كقوله: كل خط قام على خط؛ فإن الزاويتين الحادثتين على جنبيه إما قائمتان أو متساويتان لهما. قوله ومحمولاتها أي محمولات المسائل أمور خارجة عنها أي عن موضوعات المسائل لاحقة لها أي عارضة لتلك الموضوعات، والمراد ههنا محمولة عليها؛ فإن العارض هو الخارج المحمول، فإذا أجرد عن قيد الخروج للتصريح به فيما قبل بقي الحمل، ولو اكتفى المصنف باللحوق لكفى، ويوجد في بعض النسخ. قوله لذواتها: وهو بحسب الظاهر لا ينطبق إلا على العرض الأولي، أي اللاحق للشيء أولا بالذات، أي بدون واسطة في العروض، ولا يشمل العارض بواسطة المساوي مع أنه من العرض الذاتي اتفاقا، ولذا أوله بعض الشارحين وقال: أي لاستعداد مخصوص بذواتها، سواء كان لحوقه إياها لذواتها أو لأمر يساويها؛ فإن اللاحق للشيء لما هو هو يتناول الأعراض الذاتية جميعا على ما قال المصنف في شرح الرسالة الشمسية. ثم إن هذا القيد يدل على أن المصنف اختار مذهب الشيخ في لزوم كون محمولات المسائل أعراضا ذاتية لموضوعاتها، وإليه ينظر كلام شارح المطالع، لكن الأستاذ المحقق أورد عليه أنه كثيرا ما يكون محمول المسألة بالنسبة إلى موضوعها من الأعراض العامة الغريبة كقول الفقهاء كل مسكر حرام. لا ينطبق أي: قوله "لاحقة لها لذواتها" غير منطبقة على الغير، وأن العرض قسمان: أولي، وغير أولي كما مر بيانه في أول الكتاب، وإنما قال بحسب الظاهر لما يجيء من التأويل. (برهان) وقال في بيان التأويل يعني أول قوله: لذواتها بعض الشارحين أن اللزوم ههنا القابلية المخصوصة بذوات الموضوعات والاستعداد هو كون الشيء بالقوة القريبة أو البعيدة من الفعل. سواء كان الحوقه أي لحوق العرض الذاتي المحمول لذوات الموضوعات، أي بلا واسطة أمر كالتعجب اللاحق للإنسان أو للأمر الذهني ليساوي ذلك الأمر لموضوعات المسائل كالضحك العارض للإنسان بواسطة التعجب والمساوي. والحاصل أن لحوق الأمور الخارجة للموضوعات لاستعداد بالذاتي شامل لكلا اللحوقين إلى اللحوق بلا واسطة واللحوق بواسطة الأمر المساوي؛ لأن اللاحق للشيء بما هو هو أي باستعداد يتأتى في ذات ذلك الشيء يشمل جميع الأعراض الذاتية التي عندهم وهي التي تعرض بلا واسطة أي بلا واسطة أمر مساو للمعروض؛ ومن هذا البيان ظهر أن قوله : "فإن اللاحق للشيء إلخ" علة للتعميم الحاصل من قوله: سواء كان لحوقه إلخ يعني إنما عممنا اللاحق لاستعداد مخصوص بذواتها؛ لأن هذا واللاحق للشيء لما هو هو مترادفان، وهذا اللاحق الآخر يتناول جميع الأعراض الذاتية واللاحق الأول أيضاً. فإن اللاحق علة للتعميم الحاصل من قوله : سواء كان إلخ. في شرح الرسالة الشمسية: المشهور بالسعدية يعني أن المنصف في شرحه لما جعل هذا الكلام أعني اللاحق للشيء ما هو هو شاملا للأعراض الذاتية جميعا فالصواب أن يحمل قوله لذواتها في التهذيب المنسوب إليه على ذلك ويجعل شاملا لها جميعا. كل مسكر حرام فإن محمول هذه المسئلة حرام، وهو عارض للمسكر بواسطة كونه منهيا عنه وهو السكر موجود في الدم والبول وغير ذلك.
Conclusion. Parts of the sciences and their predicates are matters external to them, attaching to them for their essences. Or of its type with the essential accident like his saying: Every line standing on a line; for the two angles occurring on its sides are either right or equal to them. His saying: 'and their predicates' means the predicates of the problems are matters external to them, i.e., external to the subjects of the problems, attaching to them, i.e., accidental to those subjects, and the intent here is predicated upon them; for the accident is the external predicate. So when the qualification of externality is removed due to its being mentioned previously, the predication remains, and if the author had sufficed with 'attaching' it would have sufficed, and it exists in some copies. His saying: 'for their essences': and that apparently applies only to the primary accident, i.e., that which attaches to a thing first by essence, i.e., without intermediary in the attachment, and does not include the accident by means of a co-extensive [attribute] even though it is from the essential accident by agreement. Therefore some commentators interpreted it saying: i.e., for a specific preparedness in their essences, whether its attachment to them is for their essences or for a matter equal to them; for that which attaches to a thing insofar as it is itself includes all essential accidents according to what the author said in his commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. Then this condition indicates that the author chose the doctrine of the Master [al-Sheikh] regarding the necessity that the predicates of the problems be essential accidents for their subjects, and towards this points the speech of the commentator of al-Matali', but the verified professor objected to him that often the predicate of a problem is with respect to its subject from the general foreign accidents, like the saying of the jurists: Every intoxicant is forbidden. 'Does not apply' means his saying 'attaching to them for their essences' does not apply to other [than primary], and that the accident is of two divisions: primary and non-primary as was elucidated earlier in the book, and he only said 'apparently' because of the interpretation that follows. (Proof) And he said in explaining the interpretation, meaning first his saying: 'for their essences', some commentators [said] that the necessity here is the specific receptivity in the essences of the subjects and the preparedness is the thing being in proximate or remote potentiality to actuality. Whether its attachment, i.e., the attachment of the essential accident which is the predicate to the essences of the subjects, i.e., without an intermediary thing like wonder attaching to man, or by a mental matter equal to that matter for the subjects of the problems like laughter accidental to man by means of wonder and the equal. And the result is that the attachment of external matters to the subjects due to essential preparedness includes both attachments: the attachment without intermediary and the attachment by means of the co-extensive matter; because that which attaches to a thing insofar as it is itself, i.e., by a preparedness established in the essence of that thing, includes all essential accidents according to them, which are those that attach without intermediary, i.e., without an intermediary matter equal to the subject; and from this explanation it appears that his saying: 'for that which attaches to a thing etc.' is the cause for the generalization resulting from his saying: 'whether its attachment etc.' meaning we only generalized 'that which attaches due to a specific preparedness in their essences' because this and 'that which attaches to a thing insofar as it is itself' are synonyms, and this latter includes all essential accidents and the former also. For 'that which attaches' is the cause for the generalization resulting from his saying: 'whether etc.' In the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya: the famous one as al-Sa'diyya means that the author in his commentary, since he made this statement, I mean 'that which attaches to a thing insofar as it is itself', inclusive of all essential accidents, then the correct thing is to interpret his saying 'for their essences' in al-Tahzeeb attributed to him accordingly and to make it inclusive of all of them. Every intoxicant is forbidden, for the predicate of this problem is 'forbidden', and it is accidental to the intoxicant by means of it being prohibited, and intoxication exists in blood and urine and other than that.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the parts of sciences and their predicates. It begins by stating that the predicates of scientific problems are matters external to the subjects, attaching to them 'for their essences' (li-dhatiha). The author then clarifies the meaning: 'predicates' (mahmulat) refers to what is predicated on the subjects, and the term 'external' (kharija) emphasizes that these are accidental attributes. The key point is the interpretation of 'li-dhatiha'. The commentator notes that apparently this phrase only includes primary accidents (al-'arad al-awwali) which attach without any intermediary. But it would exclude accidents that attach via a co-extensive attribute (al-'arad bi-wasita al-musawi), even though those are considered essential accidents by agreement. Therefore, some commentators reinterpreted 'li-dhatiha' to mean 'due to a specific preparedness in the subjects' essences', which includes both primary accidents and those mediated by a co-extensive attribute. This reinterpretation is supported by quoting that 'that which attaches to a thing insofar as it is itself' includes all essential accidents. The passage then discusses that this shows the author (of Tahzeeb) chose the position that the predicates must be essential accidents. However, a professor objected that sometimes the predicate is a general foreign accident (al-'arad al-'amm al-gharib), like the example from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Here 'forbidden' is a predicate that belongs to a general category, not specific to intoxicants. The commentator resolves this by explaining that the preparedness interpretation covers such cases as well, because 'forbidden' attaches to intoxicant due to its specific preparedness (being intoxicating) even though the forbiddance is a general attribute. The passage concludes by referring to a commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya that confirms this inclusive understanding. The example of 'every intoxicant is forbidden' illustrates that the predicate 'forbidden' is accidental to 'intoxicant' via the co-extensive attribute of prohibition, and intoxication itself can be found in other things like blood and urine, so it is not co-extensive with intoxicant.

Key Terms

عارض
لذاتها
العرض الذاتي
العرض العام الغريب

Key Takeaway

General Foreign Accidents as Essential: The Fiqh Example
Author defines lazim and arad, clarifies 'li-dhatiha' as including essential accidents and those mediated by co-extensive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general foreign accident and explain why it can still be considered an essential accident for a specific subject under the broad interpretation of 'li-dhatiha'.

Core Idea
A general accident like 'forbidden' can be essential for 'intoxicant' due to its specific preparedness, even though it applies to other things.

The passage introduces a fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' The predicate 'forbidden' is a general foreign accident, yet the commentator argues it is still an essential accident for 'intoxicant' because it attaches via a co-extensive attribute (prohibition) following from intoxicant's preparedness. This lesson explores how general accidents fit into essential predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In the statement 'كل مسكر حرام', the subject is 'intoxicant' and the predicate is 'forbidden'.
This is a universal affirmative proposition.
2
Classify the predicate
The predicate 'forbidden' is a general accident because it applies not only to intoxicants but also to blood, urine, etc. It is 'al-'arad al-'amm al-gharib'.
The passage notes that intoxication exists in blood and urine, so 'forbidden' is not exclusive.
3
Determine the mode of attachment
The predicate attaches to the subject via the co-extensive attribute of prohibition (النهي), which is co-extensive with intoxicant in terms of ruling.
The passage says: 'عارض للمسكر بواسطة كونه منهيا عنه'.
4
Apply the preparedness interpretation
Under the broad interpretation, 'forbidden' is an essential accident of intoxicant because it follows from intoxicant's specific preparedness (being intoxicating), even though it is a general accident.
The commentator's reinterpretation of 'li-dhatiha' covers such cases.
